# 5

# Not the Same
> > Walking tall, you'd bought it all
> > And you were not the same after that
> > Till someone died on the waterslide
> > And you were not the same after that
> > 
## Addie, 2020

Our official plurality journey began with pizza during the pandemic.

COVID was at a lull in August of 2020, and restraints on gathering loosened for a brief summer window while cases were low. Importantly, _people could go out to eat_. After months of being cooped up in our Chicago condo we were desperate for any hint of normality, so we jumped on the chance to get pizza at our favorite Chicago tavern. 

It had been a hot summer, and we'd spent it swimming in the lake as well as doing a lot of work on ourselves. Gone were the long nights of drinking, replaced with a healthy diet and more early morning activities. I was in the best shape of my adult life after a half-year of working with a personal trainer. The suicidal ideation was gone, replaced with morning meditations, tea, and a deep feeling of being _centered_ that I had never felt before.

The area we lived in bristled with residential high-rises, mom and pop restaurants, and families living out their lives in middle-income housing. It was the sort of community that pulls you in deep and *forces* you to care; where your neighbors were the same people who served you at the pub, or the ones who ran the register at the small grocer nearby, or lived in your building. It was home, in a way that I had never experienced before.

Safe. _I was safe._

As we walked to the tavern my mind drifted back to memories of my transition. I had lost my job due to a layoff and had just enough money to pay for next month's rent. I had a religious experience in that moment: a guardian spirit assured me things would be okay and started taking over my body. She helped me find a new job, scrap to start my career, and navigate being estranged from my family.  

Yep. You heard that right. Guardian Angel. Her name was Tally, and she saved my life.

A chilly breeze blasted us with the smells of the lake as we moved between high-rises. So yes, when my dysphoria was at its worst and I was ready to give up and die I was, quite literally, possessed by the spirit to survive.

I cannot overstate this; I'd wanted to kill myself since I was about 13, and every single day I didn't attempt was a blessing. Having Tally believe in me, holding my hand through the toughest two years of my life, saved me. I would have died without her, easily.

Back in the real world the traffic light changed and we crossed the four-lane street. I squeezed D's hand and then, without meaning to, words started coming from my mouth:

"Remember the spirit I told you about?"

There it was, that "I'm no longer in control" feeling. Saying the words felt... Involuntary? Compelled? Puppeted? I didn’t want to talk but there I was, talking anyway. That happened to me a lot when I was young: words would just come out of my mouth before my brain thought through them, and then I’d get in trouble for it. I could never remember little outbursts, come to think of it. 

But this? This feeling of _watching_ as my body went through the motions? This was different.

My “lips moved without my will. “Yeah. Tally. The one that did transition for me."

"The fox spirit that took control of your body for a while?"

"Yeah. That one."

We had talked about this at some point. I had long since chalked it up to either a spiritual experience or a mental break. Either way, it was in the past. 

“I thought she was gone.”

My head felt fuzzy, disconnected. I wanted to wake up.

_This wasn't supposed to happen anymore._

I watched my body cross the street, turn to D, and flash them a bright smile. “Surprise, she’s still here!”

D leaned on my shoulder. After five years of marriage we still acted like newlyweds, all playful and affectionate and such. We'd been through a lot together; the death of their father, panic attacks, depression, layoffs, and we got through by being each other's cheerleader. Our first date was seeing "Silver Linings Playbook" and spending the walk home talking about our traumas and our mental illnesses, so we took mental health pretty seriously in this house.

“That’s neat,” they said.

I noticed, as they grabbed my arm, just how much stronger they had gotten in the past three months.mWe had spent that time working through their gender. They started low dose testosterone a few months ago and just... gosh. They _blossomed_. Between their transition and my work to recover from the attempt in 2019 we were closer than ever.

For a brief moment I could move my mouth. And in a panic, I tried to tell D I was not okay. But all that came out was her voice, her choices, her _will._

“I don’t know what this means,” I managed, and then I lost control again.

“Who knows?” They looked to the sky for a moment. "I wish I could thank her for keeping you safe back then."

Words just spilled out of me. "Well, you're in luck then."

Their eyes widened. "Really? I thought she went away in 2011."

"I did, yeah. But I like to check in from time to time."

D stopped to meet my eyes.

"Tally?"

"Speaking!"

This was about the point where my memory faded out. Like, I remembered walking two blocks, the pizza we ate, the cosmo Tally ordered. But the memory was all fuzzy. Imagine trying to watch a scrambled porn channel in a hotel room and trying to make sense of what you were seeing. It was sort of like that; I knew things happened, but they didn't happen to *me*.

Guess I have to pass this over to Tally to finish, then.

## Tally, 2020

To begin with, I’m surprised I didn't go on a date with D earlier.

I'd poked my nose in to check on Addie from time to time, and I'd spoken to D a few times in the past without them knowing. But the idea of going out for dinner and a drink just never occurred to me until Addie started to get off the sauce. She's a bit of an anxious worrywort and never liked interventions, so I never tried before.

Now, though? Addie was safe. It was okay for me to give her a gentle push.

We arrived at the tiny tavern to a hero’s welcome. Gino's North was our local haunt pre-pandemic. It was this little shotgun bar that sat 10 to 20 people, tops, and stayed alive on the strength of their grandmother’s pizza recipe. Old gays owned the place and decorated it with plenty of camp and charm; local artists' work dotted the walls, and a giant faux marble statue of a nude woman stood sentinel over the bar. This place was so important to us that we made a point during the pandemic to order delivery from them every week to help keep them alive when restaurants and offices were closed down. It was the sort of place you expect to see in a sitcom: everyone knows your name, your hobbies, and your favorite orders, and nobody gets mean with that information.

We settled into one of four round booths beside the bar. Plexiglass surrounded us on two sides, but that didn't bother me at all. It was just nice to be up in the front and, well, _existing_. I know that's odd to say but that's exactly how I felt. A few moments ago it was 2008, and now it’s 2020.

(This is a completely and totally normal human experience, right? Anybody?)

The bartender brought water over. We made small talk from behind our masks for a moment, and I pretended to be Addie, the “normal” one, so nobody would catch wise.

"I didn't realize you came back," D said in a hushed voice. They had this gobsmacked look in their eyes that just made them look cute as a button.

I guess it's not every day your spouse blinks and becomes a completely different person, but they sure were along for the ride.

"Well, sweetheart, I never went away. Just went quiet for a little while."

"You've never talked to me before, though."

I chuckled. "Okay, a _long_ while. But I'm here now! You wanted to thank me, right? Let's get drinks first. You know, I've never had a drink in my life and Austen says the drinks here are great!"

That was Addie's name back before system discovery. Austen. The body's name. Poor dear didn't realize we existed back then, even though she'd experienced actual freaking multiplicity in her past. Stubborn one, her. We collectively decided she had to change her name so that no one alter was so important that they got to use “our” name.

It’s weird, I know. But that’s how our brain works.

"Yeah... uh, right. Thank you! Let's do that.” We waved down the server. I ordered a cosmo. Not sure why; Addie never really liked them to start with. Maybe it was the rom-coms I watched while Addie and I shared a body at the start of our transition. The ladies in those films loved ordering cosmos, and I was a lady, so a cosmo it was.

I ran my finger over the rim of the glass and flashed a wicked smile. "You should know that Addie is watching over my shoulder and is just fit to be tied!"

"Thanks for keeping them alive," D said. We toasted and took a sip. Turns out, not a bad idea ordering that cosmo. Real tasty.

"It was nothing. Couldn't have them hurting the body we share, after all."

"What brings you back?"

I put down the drink. "They're ready to know about us."

That caught D by surprise. I folded my hands and waited for them to process.

"Us?"

"Yep. _Us_."

"You can't be serious."

"Things are going to get rocky," I said as I tapped my forehead. "Up here, that is. But I know they're gonna get through it as a team. Doubly so with someone like you at their side."

The air felt thick and time slowed to a crawl. We sat in that silence for what felt like forever.

"That's a lot to take in," D said.

"They have no idea what's coming," I said, "and they're going to need you."

"Of course! I love you. …uh, them.”

"We made the best choices," I grasped their hands. "Meeting you, marrying you, and loving you. It's all gonna be okay. Promise. May just be a while before we get there."

"Sure thing," D said. “But first, pizza.”

## Addie, 2020

The L train rumbled overhead. College kids chatted about partying as they passed us on the sidewalk. High rises surrounded us on all sides, as if the city was a gigantic cradle holding us in safety.

I came to as we walked home. It's... hard to explain with words. It feels like a flashbulb firing in your head, or like waking up from a light nap. I knew that we just had dinner and yet when I tried to recall details about the past hour the memories slipped out of my fingers.

D squeezed my hand. I felt tipsy, and then memories of cocktails flooded my brain. Gauging by their wide smile and silly stride they were solidly in the pocket too. 

"Tell Tally I said thanks," D said. "It was nice meeting her."

"Tally, huh? She hasn't been around in ages—"

That was the moment I felt her presence inside. Warm, calming, motherly in its own way. I swear to you, I could feel her hand on my shoulder, her breath on my neck. Images of calm forests and night skies flooded my brain. For the first time in a decade, Tally was back.

_Tally was back._

"Holy shit."

D and I rounded the street corner in silence. Inside, however, it was a joyous reunion. I imagined myself hugging Tally, her warmth enveloping my body. She smelled of lavender. She was... blonde, I think. Milky skin. Green eyes. Two giant fox ears on top of her head, and a tail that wrapped around me as we hugged.

D turned to me. "You okay?"

"I thought you were gone," I said. D shot me a look but the words weren’t meant for them.

I could hear Tally’s laughter in my head. "You don't have to talk to me out loud. You remember that much, right?"

I pursed my lips and, uh, pushed my voice inward. Sorry. There's no good way to describe that without seeming like you've got a screw loose, is there? I was talking to someone inside my head, and they were talking right back to me. Totally normal.

I thought, "Uh. Yeah. Right. It's been forever, hasn't it?"

Tally flashed an image of her stern face in my head. "You said you didn't need me anymore, so I left."

"Oh."

She crossed her arms. "Yeah. *Oh.*”

"Still with me, Austen?" The world snapped into too-sharp focus. D and I were walking into our condo's lobby. They had both of my hands in theirs and a worried look on their face.

"I think so."

"Looked like nobody was home for a second there."

"I'm... gosh. I can't believe I"m saying this, but I'm talking to Tally.”

“I know, hon.” they smiled. “Everything okay?"

"Better than okay," I said. "Everything is wonderful!"

"I'll leave you two to it, then."

The world fell out of focus again and I found myself with Tally. I swear to you, I wanted to break into tears right there and then. She was back! My friend, my protector, my other half... Finally, we were whole again!

I imagined holding her hands in mine as we danced to the music of angels.

"I'm so sorry, Tally.”

"I know." Her voice came in stronger this time: angelic, mysterious, a little breathy. “Just leave it to me. I can get us through this year but you have to let me in and trust me. It's sort of like puppetry—“

“Yes.” I interrupted her. “Whatever it is, yes. Anything for you. I would have died without you."

"_We_ would have died, Addie. But here you are, good job, amazing spouse, house. Not a bad way to run a railroad if you ask me."

For a moment I couldn't speak, couldn't move, couldn't take my eyes off her. I wanted this image burned into my memory, and if I didn’t focus hard enough the memory will slip away. And just... gosh. I missed this. Feeling her presence, knowing that someone else was in my head, watching out for me even in my darkest moments.

Tally pulled away from my grasp. "You're doing great. I just wanted to hang back long enough to say that to you. Spare me a thought every once in a while, eh?"

I rushed back into arms and buried my face in her shoulder. I felt her hug me back, her thoughts filling full of lavender, fresh spring dew, fireflies blinking in the dusk.

"I wish you wouldn't go."

“Addie—“

“_Please. Please, for the love of the gods and all that is good in this world, don't go._”

She pinched my cheek. "Oh, Addie, I’m not going anywhere. You're stuck with me! Besides, I don't think you'll ever be alone in here again."

"Come again?"

"Spoilers," she said. With a snap of her fingers I catapulted back into the real world. 

I came to with my body tucked into bed, but I felt Tally laying right there beside me, her hands stroking my hair until I went to sleep.


## Twi, 2020

This is going to sound strange, but it's a lot easier for me to spill my guts to random people on the internet than it is for me to confide in friends. Between that longstanding quirk and not knowing any plural people at the time, I threw a hail mary of an ask to every nerd I knew:

"Hey folks, I'm not doing well lately. Like, really not well. So I'm trying something I'm usually very bad at: asking for support.

"Without going into the nitty gritty (and a wall of text), I'm dealing with some pretty nasty dissociative disorder issues that flared up in the past month. Specifically, I've gone from having a single person in my head (completely normal, I think!) to having, uh, six people in my head, give or take, depending on the day.”

(I stopped mid-word while typing the message. Six? _Six?_ It was three this morning!)

“This is causing me problems like dissociative meltdowns (where I just sort of, uh, shut down for a minute or two), panic attacks, and amnesia when a head-mate decides to ‘switch’ in and take over the body. Overall, it's a good step toward healing, as those head-mates have memories and skills that I usually don't have access to, but it doesn't change the fact that this is some scary-ass shit.

"Some folks here already know what's going on, and they've been lovely in supporting me. But it's a lot. I'd love to say that I'm handling it like a champ but I'm not. I'm white-knuckling my way through my days and feeling pretty scared about what the future holds, given these issues. 

“I’m the kind of person who takes pride in having their shit together and having a good memory, two things that are definitely not happening when six different people are trying to be a complete, single person. (That it's starting to affect my job performance is definitely not helping matters either.)

"I know times are tough right now for everyone, mental health wise, but any kind words, love, or support you have to spare would be very much appreciated right now. I also feel better when I'm helping others, so if there's anything you need to unload, problems you need help solving, or issues I can help fix, please reach out. \<3

"...and please feel free to ask questions, here or in DMs. Talking about the specifics of it makes it feel less scary, oddly enough."

Our friends showed up for us and put us in touch with people in the plural community.

I know, I know, that shouldn't be shocking. Giving a shit is table stakes in a friendship, but there I was, feeling relief and overwhelming gratitude that the people in my life took the time to care for me.

You’d almost think I was worth loving, given how they all came through!

## Addie, 2020

We can’t ignore the elephant in the room: 2020 was a very, very important presidential year. As a trans woman in a lesbian marriage, I was terrified. Politics used to be fun, but this felt existential; that if something didn’t change, my way of life was going to be on the chopping block. The one thing I want most in this world is for the election to be over.

Waking up those days started with a long bout of news: Washington Post, The Bulwark, Twitter. Click all the links around election coverage. A half hour would pass and then I’d roll out of bed. We hadn't kept up on chores for a few weeks now; doing anything other than working and living was just impossible.

No clothes. I sniffed t-shirts from the hamper. "Four more days," I said. D stirred in their bed. "That's all we have to survive before election day. Then this whole mess will be over."

"Here's hoping,” D said. “By the way, you left a note to yourself last night.”

Taped to my computer monitor was a note card in someone else’s handwriting.

"Check your videos."

“I didn’t write this.”

D shrugged. “Well, one of you did." They busied themselves with getting dressed for their court call in what we called our “pandemic mullet”: tank top, lawyerly blazer, and pajama pants. I worked in IT project management but dressed mostly the same: professional on top, pajamas on bottom.

"I don't remember writing this."

“You do.”

Another voice in my head. I was getting used to hearing them by that point. Mostly it was Tally inside, yammering away, but sometimes I'd hear different voices. Some whispered. Others screamed. Some cried. But when I felt like I was losing my cool the voices would die down and Tally would be right there, ready to calm me.

D leaned back into the room as they threw on their blazer. "Everything okay?"

"Yeah. Just... you know."

I tapped my temple, twirled my finger to sign "crazy", and rolled my eyes.

"I see," D said. "Well, make sure they make you eat breakfast." They disappeared downstairs to start their court call, and in the silence Tally hummed a little tune that I swear I could hear in the real world.

She clucked our tongue. “You don't have to be mean to me,” I… I mean Tally said. The mouth wasn’t mine to use in right now.

"What?" I thought.

“Like what you’re experiencing isn’t real.”

“It’s not that.”

“No. You want some logical rationalization so that you can convince yourself I’m not real. That way, you can dismiss me.”

“Where is this coming from?”

Tally did her best impersonation of my voice. “I’m crazy, and if I’m crazy no one will love me! But people do love me, which means I can’t possibly be crazy. Ergo, I can just ignore the voice _literally taking control of my body_ like it’s no big deal.

She grinned. “Did I get close?”

I wanted to tell her to fuck off but I still didn’t have control of my mouth. A quick look at the clock told me I was too late to make tea, or meditate, or any of the good live/laugh/love bullshit habits I’d been working on over the past few months.

So before I could get lost in the despair of being a legitimately crazy person, I put on a face and went to work.

On went the work laptop; I went about logging in and joining my first meeting of the day. When you do Agile project management your day is just meetings upon meetings, and today was supposed to be no different. Four hours of meetings in the morning, two in the afternoon, and four hours of paperwork to fill out before I could relax tonight.

I watched my team give project updates through Zoom. Then, without warning, their voices went fuzzy. I looked to the post-it note again and, without thinking, picked it up.

“You need to watch this video,” Tally said.

“I hate when you do that,” I said.

“Do what?”

“Control my arm like that. it feels creepy.”

She tutted. “You used to think it was a cool superpower.”

“That was before I had a job I had to focus on. This is a huge opportunity for me, you know? If I do this right I can become an Agile Coach full time!”

The meeting had gone quiet, but I was too busy arguing with a voice in my head to notice. 

“—Austen?”

The world snapped back into focus.

You ever have that dream where you’re taking a test and you can’t remember any of the answers? Well, I lived that dream every time this happened. Panic gripped my throat. I wanted to apologize, disconnect from the meeting, run, and hide under my covers, I was so scared.

“I’m sorry,” I said, flustered. I fought the instinct to run and hide. “Uh, I was multitasking. Come back to me in a minute?”

“Sure thing,” a coworker said, and then I smashed the mute button.

I placed the note back on the monitor bezel. “I told you I’d look at this later. Four more days, I promise.”

“When you're not a nervous wreck about the election?”

“Well, yeah. I’m too busy to have a breakdown right now, Tally. We both know that. Just let me push it down a little longer.”

For a moment there was quiet - blessed, beautiful quiet! Then her voice boomed in my ear: “Nope. you said that in 2008. Never again. I’m putting my foot down.”

My hands moved outside of my control. I shut down the work laptop. Turned off the monitor. Then, my hand reached for the phone and pulled up my photo library.

Inside were a half-dozen videos of myself that I had no memory of creating. Weirder still, none of those videos were made by *me* Sure, it was my body in the frame, and my mouth making the sounds, but something was off. One version of myself in the phone had a gentle voice, a feminine "pop" to their hips, and a warm smile that I swear to you, had you paid me a hundred dollars on the spot to replicate it, I couldn't. In another video we used a male voice that we hadn’t attempted in 12 years. Another one was my body, but it looked like it was shot by a five year old; their twinkling, bright eyes and a wide smile belied a playful, childish innocence.

Each video had the same message:

"You're doomscrolling again," the video said, and immediately I wanted to turn it off.

I didn’t remember doing this. Oh gosh, I don't remember this at all. It's me-but-not-me in the video and I'm talking to myself and this is how it ends, isn't it, when I finally crack and go crazy like I always knew would happen. After years of fighting off the insanity this was the straw that'd break everything and...

Wait, I _quit the meeting?_ Gods, I have to go back right now—

Tally’s soothing voice echoed in my ear. “Please, Stop doing that. Take a breath. Relax. I'm trying to help you, Addie. How does your chest feel?”

My shoulders relaxed. I put one hand to my heart as the other pressed the pause button on the video. My breaths were shallow and fast; my heart pounded loudly enough to drown out the background noise around me.

“You're having a panic attack, friend. Remember what your therapist says? ‘Ride the wave.’ You're going to be fine.”

I breathed until I didn’t feel dizzy anymore and restarted the videos.

The not-mes smiled and flashed warm, empathetic, confident eyes in the videos. It was almost like they were, well, _concerned_. Which didn’t make sense; who in the hell shoots a video of themselves to stage an intervention—

…oh no.

Another voice: this one husky, feminine, but familiar. I’d used it for a few months in 2016 when I had a hard butch phase. “The election will happen no matter how you end up feeling about it. Also hello, this is what it looks like when Max fronts."

I felt terror well up in my heart. why was I doing this? Surely it was an act. All I had to do was assert my will hard enough. Tell the voices in my head to buzz off. Concentrate really hard on bottling up the feelings welling up in me: terror, anger, frustration, grief, the whole lot.

“What is all this?” I hissed. I wanted to delete it all and throw the phone away, but I couldn’t bring myself to do it.

“I’d like to wake up now,” I said, but the words wouldn’t leave my lips.

Tally rooted through my phone. She went on a application deleting spree: Twitter, Facebook, my news aggregators. Anything I could use to obsess about the election, Tally nuked it from the phone.

"You need to take a break," she said, using my mouth to say it. "For all our sakes, you need to give us some control. The more you fight, the worse this will get."

I felt terrified, powerless, and humbled, all in equal measure. So I gave up fighting as the body went about de-politicizing my life.

“You’re going on a social media diet. No more doomscrolling. No more dancing around the facts of your brain. There’s a lot of us in here, and you’re going to learn to deal with that.”

“I have a therapist.” I said. For a moment I looked back to the work laptop and willed myself, with every fiber of my being, to jump back on the work call. I might as well have tried to move the Earth itself.

"You need to tell him about us. Today. Either do it at your pace, or I'll get it done for you right now.”

She pulled up my therapist’s number.

“Your choice.”

Then: vertigo, disorientation, the fulttering of eyelashes and the panic of possibly passing out.

I woke up. Again.

My hands were back under my own control. I turned them around to inspect them closely, making sure I wasn't dreaming. My mind felt clean - too clean, as if I had forgotten something very important that I couldn't quite put my finger on.

“Glad to see you getting the point,” Tally said. “Now take the day off. we’re getting you an emergency session.”

## Joey (or one of us)?
\<Something here about that emergency sesion.
“I have not done this before. But we’re gonna get through it.”

## Addie 2020

I didn’t just rely on a therapist.

My entire life, up to that point, had been shaped by the kindness of internet strangers. I learned to write by way of a Tennessee autoworker who published science fiction. I cut my teeth writing for strangers on the internet, taking and giving praise for the stories we wrote together. Even now, 30 years later, I still keep in contact with many of the folks I met there, and when times get tough I still look to them for support.

The plural community, as it turns out, was no different.

The Great Cats System was one of the first systems to help us figure out being plural, and honestly their collective help saved our life. We spent days chatting with them over Telegram and for the longest time I only knew their face by the ever-changing, furry avatar picture that represented who I was talking to. But the intensity of their attention, the obvious love and empathy they put into every person they helped, and the way their experience of DID matched up with the way we experienced it kept me coming back every minute of every day, hanging onto any advice they could give me.

They were the first system I really got to know in detail, and let me tell you the experience of beeing seen like that was liquid lightning in my veins. In those early conversations my memory felt like swiss cheese, and scrolling back through the timeline would leave me scratching my head as to who wrote our responses. She assured us that all was well, and that this was part of the process.

It didn't take her long to prescribe a fix for my problems: "You need to take a vacation, Addie."

"I don't have PTO," I replied.

"No. _You_, Addie. You need to take some time away from the front."

I'd made a study of all the lingo: switching, co-consciousness, littles, trauma holders, protectors, persecutors... not that I knew how to identify them inside my own head, that is, but I tried anyway. Inside was always radio silence.

"You need to let someone else front. The election is driving you up a wall."

"I don't know how."

"Ask inside. _Nicely_ ask inside, I mean. Open yourself up to them."

"Okay."

"Tell them you want to switch out," she texted, but before I could respond the world fell out of focus again.


---- 


I came to months later.

D and I were seated at a local brunch place's patio, champagne flutes in our hands. People shouted and cheered from their balconies. Every passing car honked their horn. Some even unfurled an American flag and blasted Bon Jovi over their car stereos. The owner of the joint walked around and clinked glasses with every patron.

D must have noticed me spacing out and took my free hand into theirs. "Hi! Who's this?"

“It’s Addie. Why am I out here?"

"It's November 7," D said. "The election was called. Biden won. We're gonna finish brunch and go to Boystown. Remember?"

"No?"

"It's fine. Tally said that might happen.” D raised their glass to me. “To this whole mess being over.”

“To new beginnings,” I replied, and we clinked glasses.

## Tally, 2020

Addie sat cross-legged on the beige carpet of our guest bedroom, breathing slowly, deliberately. The real world melted and she found herself staring at a barren expanse, darkness as far as the eye could see.

"So now what, Tally? This seems so silly."

"Hush! It's visualization. We need a way to talk to each other inside."

"I can hear you just fine."

"We need _something_. Like... like a talisman, you know? Something to help us conceive of _us_ Something comfortable. Something safe."

"The arcade."

My ears perked. "Okay, you have my attention. Go on."

"Well, I remember loving the arcade growing up. There was this one near us called Putt Putt. The one on Washington Street? Our uncle took us there on Saturdays for golf and go-karting. And when we were in that space, no matter who was with us, nothing bad happened to us, right?"

I smiled at her. “Because lots of kids plus lots of adults means lots of witnesses..."

"Exactly."

I put a finger to my chin and considered talking more about the uncle. No. Not yet.

“Love the energy, Addie. But let’s pick something more recent, maybe?”

“Okay.” She snapped her fingers. “Logan Arcade. We've been there, what, twice a month for the past five years to play in that pinball league? I bet we could remember every twist of that place if we needed to."

I raised my hands and well-worn wood flooring rose up to meet us. Above, pressed tin ceiling tiles fell into place with an audible clack. Brick rose from the void to define the walls. Small lights twinkled in the ceiling, and from the floor dozens of pinball machines rose into existence. Jake Shears played over the sound system and for a moment the stress and trauma that formed the background noise of our collective life gave way to simple, straightforward bliss.

I prodded Addie further. “Okay. What do we _need_ out of this place? Far as I'm concerned, we need a way to know who is driving the body, some places to represent being not in front, and a place to help everyone sleep. Thoughts?"

"Well, there's these double doors in the back we can use for the front. Throw up a control panel there - something with multiple joysticks and buttons and stuff—“

"—Inside Out style!"

The fronting room came into focus. To the rear, a double-door exit opened to an alley slick with rain. Across the street there was a drive-in projector screen that showed what the body's eyes saw. On one wall, we placed a low-slung leather sectional with a good view of the same screen. Pinball machines lined the other wall. Through an archway was a dark room lined with old coin-ops, and past that a dark, homey bar was bracketed by more pinball machines, an antique coke machine, and Chicago windows that looked out on a rain-slick city street.

“You can’t go back from this,” I told her.

“From what?”

“This is like hanging a giant ‘OPEN’ sign inside your head. I don’t know who will come, or how many there will be. Just… Look.” I pinched my brow.

“Something may go wrong.”

“Can’t be wrong,” Addie replied. She put a hand to my cheek. “I’m here with you.”

“Softie.” Laughing, I nudged Addie toward a cord attached to a large "OPEN" sign that sat in the window. "Want to do the honors?"

"Sure." Their finger touched the cord and then froze. They looked back. Swallowed a lump in their throat.

Addie flicked on the light. Cool blue and firey red neon shot out through the darkness. There was silence for a moment, but then a strong breeze threw open the door. Dozens of shadows - nothing more than eyes and soot and dust bunnies! - flooded into the arcade!

“There’s so many!”

“Yes!” I said. “We’re open for business!”

The sound of dozens of coin-ops and pinball machines spinning up buffeted us in a sweet symphony of chiptunes, chimes, and home.

## Addie, 2020

I typed my first post into the system chat: “Okay."

Someone in my head set up a system communication channel for us on the suggestion of some folks I followed on Twitter. I found it on my computer the day after the election call, and something inside me compelled me to click and write something. "Okay" was the best I could do.

My fingers started typing. A bot on the server proxied my message with custom avatars representing my alters. Tally’s avatar on the chat was a little cartoon fox we had picked off the internet.

I used a selfie.

Tally typed: “Okay? Just okay?"

"You win. This is real. There's more than one of us."

I expected anxiety. Fear, shock, something to prove to me that I had finally lost the last of my marbles. Instead, I felt a sense of calm. My shoulders relaxed and for the first time in a long, long while I felt like myself. Comfrotable. _Whole._

"What happens now?"

My fingers danced across the keyboard. "We get to know our neighbors. Hang out at the arcade. Ask questions. Be curious.”

“That sounds crazy.”
  
“That’s because we are,” Tally typed back, ending the message with a \<3 for extra sass.

## Addie, 2020

I was still waiting for a call from my family.

It had been a month since my first phone call with my sister and the silence was killing me. What surprised me was how desperately I wanted to see this man pay. Like, river-of-fire hatred of the man. It felt out of place, given the circumstances. Shouldn’t a good child support the family through this rough time? Offer solace and a neutral shoulder to lean on whenever it’s needed?

Instead, the fire raged on and on. All I could do was focus on putting out the brightest flames.

“You know what they’re going to say,” D warned us.

“But maybe this time will be different,” Lucy said, and I imagined her holding up the football to yank away again. “The stakes are higher now.”

I picked up the phone and rang my sister. She answered quickly.

“How the hell are you?”

“On tetherhooks. You told me last month dad was facing some serious trouble. What came of it?”

“Oh, your niece was just complaining about him,” she said.

I knew that lawyers had been called, and that the whole trouble started from a therapist’s mandatory report, but I still couldn’t find the stomach to contradict my sister.

“She made it all up.”

“Wait, everything blew over? He was accused of molesting a kid! He’s got a proven track record of assaulting women! How in the world does that all blow over?”

“She just doesn’t come around anymore,” she said. “When the family gets together, she stays home.”

The casual tone of her voice filled me with rage, and we bubbled over.

“I’m sorry, but this is in direct contradiction to what you said a month ago. You were on board with him getting his due karma. Now you’re suddenly parroting everything he says.”

“I never said that.”

“You did. I took notes.”

I could hear the surprise in her voice. “That’s weird. That’s why you don’t get to be with the family anymore.”

That’s the thing that drives me bonkers about my sister; no matter how sincere she sounds on one day, you could never expect what she’d say next. Usually it was parroting whatever line she heard last, far as I could tell, and the last person who got to her was my father.

“You still hate us for not being perfect about your transition.”

“That’s not true.”

“But it is. You refuse to come over. You hate us.“

“You’ve never once visited me in Chicago,” I replied, but that wasn’t going to be enough.

“I just wish you’d come around and stop being angry. It’s a shame that my kids won’t know their aunt because of misunderstandings like this.”

There was no reason to continue this conversation. Dad had gotten to her, and everyone was going to parrot the same answer.

“Why do you hate us so much?”

I wanted to lay it all out for her. To explain just how much being rejected for being trans hurt. About learning that being present was dangerous before the age of three. That all of the insults and anger over the years had ground me down to a smooth, round ball of a person who couldn’t get any emotional connection to the folks that hurt us. For the kid who wanted exactly one thing in the entire world - to be a girl - but couldn’t admit it for fear of being caught and sent to a straight camp.

But instead, all I could muster was resignation. “I… Sure. Yeah.”

“Can we put this behind us?”

“Sure,” I said, and promptly changed the subject to something small and Midwestern to end the meat of the conversation.

“How’d it go?” D asked.

“I don’t know why I bothered,” I replied.


## Lilith, 2024

Why would we write such an ugly book, you may ask? Because I need you to see this. To hear this. To know I am seen, and believed, and loved despite of all of it. I want you to feel what it’s like to be that little kid, barely out of diapers, who could not speak and had already learned that being present in the world was something to be feared. I want her - all of the little girls and boys in the system! - to know that they are loved, heard, and believed.

That is the most important thing I know on Earth, and all I need to know.

---

# 6





# Let’s do the Time Warp again
> > Well, I was walking down the street just a-having a think
> > When a snake of a guy gave me an evil wink
> > He shook-a me up, he took me by surprise
> > He had a pickup truck and the devil's eyes
> > He stared at me and I felt a change
> > Time meant nothing, never would again
> > 
## Max, 1994
You ever wondered what time travel would be like? I don’t because I have been there and I have done that and I can tell you, it wasn't worth it.

So picture this. It was 1994. I was in the basement of my mom's house with a dingy old MS-DOS machine. It was a rainy mid-afternoon outside, and I could hear fat drops pattering on the half-window near the ceiling. The basement was a playroom of sorts: air hockey tables, dolls, nerf guns, costumes for dressing up and making plays and such.

But for me, the ocean-blue glow of the QBASIC screen was all the toy I needed. At the time I was working on an ASCII movie, I think, where I animated little text symbols to tell a story.

Upstairs, the yelling started. It was my mom yelling at my stepsisters. Again. I tried to block out the words - easier to have plausible deniability later if I was too busy to pay attention to the argument - but the shrill tone echoed through the kitchen, off the pantry, down the stairs, and then to my ears.

"You're worthless! How *dare* you treat your father like this?"

There was silence on the other end. There was always silence on the other end. It was always mom, screaming at two kids who couldn't muster a reply. I tried typing faster, harder, begging the buckling springs to drown out the sound, but mom was too loud, too piercing.

"I don't want you ungrateful kids in my home!"

I focused on the wall. Mom had a thing about splatter paint. Really any sort of craft with paint was her home turf. The walls of the basement had this coat of white paint with splatters of black and light charcoal that swirled, struck, and slashed their way behind the computer desk. I traced some of the lines and tried to go away, somewhere, anywhere but here—

"Aww, are you gonna cry and call your Mommy now so you can bitch and moan about how evil I am?"

I didn't go upstairs. I never went upstairs. She'd yell at me the same way if I did. Instead my fingers traced the paint on the cinder-block walls: little whorls, long swipes, thin rails where I could imagine my finger was a tiny railcar riding and jumping across an endless series of tracks, far far away from the noise. There, warmed by the blue glow of code and the splatter-paint railroad tracks, I could lose myself in absurd logic and daydreams while the screaming went on and on.

## Addie, 2003

So if I’m being honest, I’m not entirely sure where I fit into the system.

I know I’ve been around since 2003 in one form or another. Maybe I was around before then, maybe I wasn’t. But when it comes to memories, the earliest one I can remember clearly is from November 2003: meeting my new best friend, Dustin Friend.

We met in my freshman year of college at a furry meetup at Purdue. It was our first time going out to meet other furs in person. I knew one, maybe two of the folks at this thing, and only in that sort of friends-of-friends way that made introductions awkward. But this was my big chance to make friends - isn’t that what you do in college? - and I wanted to come out of the gate swinging wide.

At the time the system was, uh, let’s just say very confused about things. Misty spent the first six months of school trying to express herself as a girl, and in the process got us into a bit of hot water at our all-male college. Leaving home left us exhausted, as decompressing from all the trauma we experienced was taking its toll.

The meetup took place in a student apartment belonging to a friend from the UAE who wooed us with Smash Brothers and obscure flavors of kit-kats from his home country. His girlfriend also wowed the system with lots of nerd trivia and an adorable cat. While I can’t tell you the names of all the people I met at that little party, the only thing I can remember for sure was my friend, Dustin Friend, playing endless rounds of Smash with me, as if the world around us faded into nothingness. We clicked together like jigsaw pieces, and as the night went on our conversation grew deeper and deeper. We left that party with plans to hang out as soon as humanly possible, even if it meant a 3 hour drive to his place in Northern Indiana to make it happen.

And the best part? Dustin was safe. Before we discovered our system that seemed like a given, but gosh. Twi knew any single slip-up at college could come back to haunt us: tell an acquaintance from the same hometown a little too much in passing and it’d make its way back through the small-town gossip tree to our parents. But Dustin? Dustin was from an entirely different part of the state! Nothing we confided in him had a chance of getting back to my parents.

I can’t overstate just how powerful that realization was for us.

It wouldn’t have happened with anyone else in the system. Potts would have kept his distance. Twi would have thought something was wrong. Sam… well, she might have slept with him that night. It had to be me, the boisterous and outgoing asexual alter, taking point that day.

Dustin and I got together at least once a month after that. With time and a lot of bravery I found my queer little tribe on campus and blossomed into a weekday socialite. Then, on the weekends, I’d finish class, hop in my bedraggled, blue Pontiac Sunbird, and drive the three hours to Rochester, IN for a weekend with Dustin.

Every weekend together, we played games. A lot of games.

I obsessed about pinball, he obsessed about Pac-Man. But we both loved to play Dance Dance Revolution, and the closest machine to him was Tokens-n-Tickets. So most weekends we’d squeeze in a few hours to travel to the arcade and get our game on.

We’d spend hours pumping quarters into their DDR machine. I swear, by the time he moved away from that town I had memorized every machine in that place by heart: Ikaruga near the back, Keyboardmania on the right as you walked inside, a pristine Captain Fantastic pin opened up to show its innards stored behind glass near the entrance. Upstairs, there was pizza, though we never bothered to eat there. (That was time we could have spent gaming, after all!)

Six hours round trip - seven, if you counted travel time to the arcade! - to hang with this dude. And we’d do this dance at least once a month. So when we say this guy knows us, he *really, really knows us.*

## Max, 1996

Blink.

It was 1996 and I was back at mom's house. The home was a lot quieter now. No yelling. Lots more laughter. Parties on the weekend, too. Real alcohol-fueled ragers. I was sitting on one end of an extremely-90s upholstered sectional, and mom sat on the other. She popped a beer in the silence that filled the room.

"They were just troublemakers," she says. "Aren't you glad they're gone?"

My words come out robotic. "Yes mom. they were just the worst."

I miss them. To this day, I miss them both. But I smiled and I said what I knew needed to be said because that's how you don't get abused at home.

## Addie, 2024

Sam slammed her beer glass down and stared at me with icy brown eyes.

“No, okay, seriously. who the fuck cares? You met a friend in college. So did every single other kid that ever set foot in a dorm. What’s next, telling everyone about how hard you studied for finals, or how he both got so drunk at a convention one night that he fell asleep under the hotel bathroom sink?”

I hook my head. “He’s not just anyone, honey. He was the best man at our wedding! And like I said before–”

“–You get to write your part your way, sure. But it’s not interesting. It’s not helpful. It’s not... I don’t know. Not proof that we’re a system, or some wild switching experience, or anything like that. I don’t care about this story and I live inside your head. Why should anyone care?”

“Look, I’m getting to it. honest. Twi’s not the only part in the system that knows how to write. Just trust me, okay?”

“This is why we stopped writing.” She scoffed and ordered another champagne, and I went back to the draft.

Back in Rochester, Indiana, we were going to bed in his little bedroom. I laid on a foam chair that unfolded out to a flat cushion he lovingly called the flippy-fuck. We should have been sleeping, but we were too excited;.so we stared at the ceiling and talked about nothing at all for hours.

It didn’t matter the subject; I just loved talking about… well, whatever we wanted, really. Games, life, love, our initial foray into meeting furries in the real world, and figuring out our queerness. There were no boundaries on our conversations, and no matter how weird I got, or how outside of the bounds of “normal” I was, he was there for me. Listening. Laughing with me, not at me.

A friend like this, for me, had never happened before. In spurts, maybe. I had a friend or few in high school who reached that level of trust. But they were never fully safe, you know? In a small town word travels fast, and I was far enough outside the norm, as a closeted, queer trans woman in a literal Klantown, that any tidbit of information would be premium gossip back home.
 
“I don’t know who I am,” I admitted to him. “Maybe I’m a boy, maybe I’m androgynous. Maybe I’m gay, maybe I’m straight. I just… I don’t know.”

I stared at the ceiling, dumbstruck at the words coming from my lips. I had never said the words out loud before. Sam? Sure. She was out loud and proud as an androgynous bi person on chat servers online, secure in her anonymity and the physical distance from any harm that information sharing could have.

But at that point, Dustin knew our friends both at college and back home. We’d hosted him many times for holidays and long weekends at my mom’s house. This all could have gone so badly if he slipped up and said he knew too much.

The world is a dangerous place when you grew up in a small-town gossip mill. Word (and consequences) travel fast there. But Dustin? I knew deep in my heart Dustin was safe. That he’d keep my secrets, and he’d love me even if I did change.

Dustin rolled over to meet my eyes. “You’d still be my friend.”

“It just terrifies me.”

“I know how you feel.”

“But you’d still like me?”

“You’re awesome,” he assured me. I believed him. The memory grew hazy for a moment. We were sleep-deprived, exhausted from too much DDR, and living a moment where everything was just perfect. No anger, no yelling, no fighting; just two people being vulnerable around each other, nothing to see here.

“Is that the sun?” he asked, pointing to the window. “Oh shit.”

“We just talked all night, didn’t we?”

We laughed as the grey dawn touched our face, and in that moment I knew this guy was going to be my best friend for life.

## Max, 2000

Blink.

It was the year 2000. I was in a different house this time - dad's, I think. After the divorce he had refinished a dingy pole barn into a home for us. I was alone in a bedroom that must have belonged to me, given all the books and gaming consoles and such. I was resting my elbows on sculpted carpet that left deep patterns on my arms when I lay there for a long time.

In front of me was an old CRT monitor and an open mid-size tower PC. There were burn marks on some of the parts because dad had bought things cheap at a fire sale. Said if I could build a computer from the mess, he'd let me do whatever I wanted with it. I remembered staring at black-and-grey command prompt screens for hours at a time, typing in DISKPART commands and crossing my fingers that this time would be the time Windows 98 finally decided to install.

I’d been at it for months by this point, but the reward was too sweet to give up on: a honest, genuine private space in this home. Even if it was virtual, I’d have a line to the outside while—

*Crash!*

Upstairs I heard yelling, dishes shattering, people wrestling for control. There was always noises like that from the upstairs living room, and the voices reverberated through the house thanks to the living room's vaulted, primer-coat-on-drywall, pole barn ceiling. I knew Potts and Twi grew up here, but this space wasn't my home. 

I started typing commands into the computer, working quickly as I could. I didn’t like staying here. Things got dangerous here.

I know it sounds rude in retrospect, but there were different "masks" I wore depending on which home I was in. For mom, I put on a jester's cap and acted as silly as I could, knowing that would get her praise. At Dad's, Twi and Potts had to make themselves as small a target as possible to avoid their ire. And these weren’t just fanciful moods, either; each mask had their own backstory, their own knowledge, their own _skills_, so that there could be no cross-pollination between the two homes.

Me being here wasn’t just uncomfortable; it was dangerous. If my stepmom got a hold of me and started screaming, I’d start crying, and that just makes things worse. So I redoubled my efforts on the PC, hoping I could fix Twi’s problem and go back inside my head.

These weren't my kind yelling people. When I heard yelling it meant people were too drunk at 2AM, but if I stepped back from the computer for a moment I could identify the voices and the emotions: dad, my stepmom, my stepbrothers, my sister. A fight had broken out. Something about dad putting his foot down and trying to lay down ground rules set one of my stepbrothers off. Maybe this was about a girlfriend, maybe it was some drama with their dad, maybe they were yelling about report cards or smoking at school, whatever. It wasn't something I wanted to know about in any case.

The computer. _The computer is all that matters_.

I was already a writer by this point, apparently. Twi had a secret folder on the family computer where he’d write out his fanfics. We had made contact with a few writing mentors who were helping us find our feet in longer form writing. Getting this computer set up would let us write longer, more intense things.

I don’t know why, but Twi gets a rush whenever we do this writing thing. Something about it gets him to let his hair down, you know? It’s apparently good - we’ve published a few pieces and been nominated for some awards - but he couldn’t do longer stuff like novels until we had a computer of our own to draft on.

_To feel safe on,_ he corrected me.

I was hungry, but there was a strong feeling inside of my body like I shouldn't eat, shouldn't drink. *We have to make weight for tomorrow's dual meet.* The voice wasn't coming from me but it was *of* me, if that makes sense. Hell, I don’t even know how to wrestle, but apparently someone in here is pretty good at it.

I promised the voice I wouldn’t snack. It felt relief.

I entered a command and watched progress bars for a while. I loved progress bars. You could just zone out and stare at them and the world went by a lot faster. I liked when things went fast. Fast meant you got to sleep sooner, that the house got quieter sooner.

Just a bit more work, I thought to myself. One or two more restarts. We could fix this. Just needed a few more tweaks to the PC and there we could have privacy.

My stepmom screamed for Twi with the Angry Voice and he knew better than to make her wait. My body jumped to its feet and sprinted to the closed bedroom door. The world blurred. The memory was no longer mine but I got the highlights.

I sat in a dark-stained, cherry wood chair. Above, an ornate cuckoo clock ticked away on the wall. There was an aquarium bubbling away in a corner of the room, and the sound to this day could put ice into my veins.

## Sam 2024

I put my champagne flute down and applauded.

“Okay, Addie. Fine. You’ve convinced me that Dustin is a cool dude. What next?”

“I don’t know.”

My head tilted. I blinked. “Excuse me?”

“Like, I know what happens next. And I do remember going to tons of cons with him, spending a summer together, meeting all sorts of new folks, and getting too drunk at too many crazy room parties with him to count. But then? Once we started to know and trust people? the memories get–”

“–Hazy.” I tilted my head in confusion. The words came from my mouth, but they weren’t mine.

Hazy. _Unreal_.

I lived in a dream through most of college. Then, after graduation, Dustin and I moved to Indianapolis. He and I lived in the same apartment complex and were attached at the hip. If I wasn’t at work, and if I wasn’t busy trying to make my relationship with my family work, I was over at his place playing games.

He - and Mike, and Steve, and all the other friends we had at that time - they were our rock. Stuff got weird and hazy around that time, but they supported us, and we supported them. When my local group imploded he invited me into his circle of local friends with open arms. Better - he shouted our praise to the rooftops when given the chance. I swear, best wingman ever.

The new friends didn’t see me as a burden to shoulder. No - I was amazing to them, just as every single one of them were to me. For the first time a friend group ceased being casual friends and become a family.

...oh gosh. A family. My family.

We were back in our arcade again. I expected Addie to flash me this shit-eating, joker-style grin, the kind of smile that followed a horrible pun. But instead, they reached across the table for my hands.

I... I hadn’t really taken the time to look at them before. I mean, really look at them, inside the arcade. Here they were sporting a bright pink undercut, silly glasses, and a Hawaiian shirt so loud that it could have been cited for a noise violation. They were short and lanky, with bright eyes, toned build, and the bright smile of a performer. Her mood was a coin-toss between “all business, all the time” and “let me put on a show that’ll wow everyone around us.” A class clown and an orator, both too gay to function and too queer to move out of your way, she held space in a way few of us could match.

But here, the way Addie pierced us with her sincere eyes? This was new.

“I figured out why I’m here,” Addie said to me. “Just now. And I wanted you out front so I knew you would hear it.”

I could feel my heart skipping beats, the body’s shoulders pulled taught with tension. She considered her words for a moment before giving my hands another squeeze.

“College is hazy for all of us, I think. There were a lot of us running around the headspace then, trying to keep things going. We all knew that if we screwed up, well, that was going to be that. No net.”

“We escape our family, or we die trying. There was never a middle ground.”

“Exactly. So we all did what needed to be done to get out, get safe. And that meant pushing down what we wanted to get what we needed.

“We were so used to having to watch every little thing we did, but with the furries, in those moments, with Dustin and the crew? we didn’t need that. And I think to some extent we didn’t want that baggage to bleed over when we made our first impressions. I could be new, different, better, if I just let the past be in the past. And, well.”

Addie gave my hands another squeeze. “If I didn’t let people find you, they couldn’t hurt you. And I’m so, so sorry that I pushed you down for all those years.”

“You did all of this to keep me safe?”

Addie looked to the ground. “Yup.”

“You found out the system existed and then locked me away for a decade.”

“To protect you, yes.”

“Some protection.”

“I never said it was a good explanation. You trust people. You let them into your heart, and when they get there they can start seeing the scars. I couldn’t let that happen.”

“Everyone has scars.”

“Not us!” They slapped the table. "Not to anyone on the outside! That’s how it starts, you know? You let someone see your flaws, that gives them a peek at your weaknesses. Then, they start prying at the edges. Little needling at first, testing the waters. But then the needling becomes berating, berating abuse, and then we’re right back to where we started.

“We - all of us - are here to protect you. And yeah, I went a bit overboard. But if it meant you wouldn’t get hurt, I’d pay the price gladly.”

I leaned close and hissed a whisper. “How many years do you think we lost like this? Wound up like an electrified solenoid, ready to pop at the slightest pressure? How many good times do you think we missed, how many good people we never grew close to?

“How much living did we _not_ do because of our past? I don’t want to think about it. But I want to be different now.”

## Max, 1996


Blink.

It was 1996 and I was in an arcade in some remote Indianapolis warehouse district. I was — ugh, I was wearing the *boy clothes* in this memory. Gods. Baggy cargo shorts and a graphic t-shirt. You'd have thought I was trying to disappear from the world when puberty hit. But whatever, yeah. As long as I didn’t think about the clothes it was an arcade. Nobody could have a bad time in an arcade!

So I imagined something new to wear in the memoryand that made me feel better: pleated skirt and graphic tank top. Much better. I could do that with my memories, whenever I wanted. Something didn’t fit? Just imagine it away.

So we were at a place called Block Party, which was Blockbuster’s failed attempt at getting in on the mid-90s arcade boom. We were there with, well, what _will_ be family, eventually.  My stepbrothers were there, reserved, and trying their best to pull off the "whatever" teen look to seem cool. Me, I had got too many kids inside my head to show up at an arcade and _not_ act like a caffeinated four-year-old with a free play card in the arcade?

Come to think of it, my uncle was there. He loved arcades. He worked as a social worker or something and he knew the nice lady who helped me with vision stuff at school. I hadn’t seen him for many years because dad did something in the 90s that made them not like each other. (Something about sex probably.) But he was _here_, and he was paying a lot of attention to me and the other boys. It was nice to get his attention again.

I remembered being close to him growing up. Lots of overnights at his really cool house, which was full of games to play and things to do. He was also very nice to me, I think. But here he was kind of… standoffish, for some reason? Whatever. I was at an arcade and I got to play video games. That’s all I needed.

We waited at the bar for game cards while sitting on benches that looked like pinball flippers. Beyond the linoleum-lined pub was a large, carpeted ocean of arcade machines and pins, all there ready to be explored.

I was _vibrating_ with energy.

I was scared of my stepbrothers, I think. They hit me and said mean things to me and made me feel like I wasn’t good enough boy for their time. So when time came to go out and have fun I split off, hoping to enjoy the games by myself.

Here and now, where everyone was on their very best behavior, things were nice. There were lots of people there so nobody could yell at us. And it was the kind of place where I got left alone to do my own thing, and when I did my own thing nobody ever yelled at me, so that was nice.

My uncle Justy handed me a game card and before they could give me any lip I dove straight into that arcade ocean with reckless abandon.


## Max, 2022


Blink.

I was alone in a spacious room in a house I didn’t recognize. I could hear waves crashing over some shore I didn’t know. In front of me was a large, flat object that _must_ be a computer screen, but it's impossibly thin. How did they fit the electron guns into this thing, anyway?

The world felt smaller, somehow. Besides that, everything was just _off_. There was a glass rectangle by my hands that vibrated about once a minute - again, that impossibly crisp screen! - and the objects around me just seemed sleeker, smaller, better than what I had seen before. Some of the devices didn’t even have wires!

I got the idea to look down at my body and nearly passed out from shock. My hands patted down my chest, my crotch, my hips. Everything is not what I remember. They're softer. Nicer, in a way I couldn't quite put into words. Maybe... comfortable?

No. _Girly._ No more nasty boy body!

I checked the date: December 20, 2020. I grabbed the mouse - no ball inside anymore! - and dragged the cursor across this impossibly large screen to find a chat window. I knew it was the Cats even if I don't know how I know that.

"Hello?" I type. “I don’t know what’s going on.”

"You're new, I take it?"

"I think?"

"It's cool," Lexi responded. "You have DID. That means there's more than one of you inside your head. You're safe, you're in your home, and everything is cool. How old are you?"

"Uh..."

"In your mind," Lexi responded. "Like, are you an adult, a kid, or a teen?"

"In between kid and teen? Tween?" I laughed. Well, someone laughed. Probably the adults inside, gobsmacked at what I was saying on a chat with someone who was a total stranger to me.

"Nice. What's your name, kiddo?”

“Max,” I typed. “Like Maxine, but cooler.”

A big grin dug deep dimples into my cheeks.

“Well Max, it’s a pleasure to meet you.”  

“Thanks. I'm very confused, very excited, and more than anything I need a primer on the last twenty years of technology because this shit is blowing my _mind_ right now."


---- 


You get over the time travel eventually. Can't stay mad about missing 20+ years for the rest of your life, after all! And even though my body is pushing 40 I still feel like I'm 13, still dress like I'm 13, and I live for every compliment I get on my look. I got us through, and now I got to enjoy the fruits of that labor.

Some days, I still feel like I'm in that Block Party from our youth. But I realize now that no matter how big the dissociated ocean that separates us, we're all from the same arcades, the same base components, the same life.

We are time travelers, lost in a labyrinth of our own memory. But it’s nice to look back and see, sometimes, that things weren’t so bad.

## Addie, 2023

All the while, through all the system discovery, confusion, processing, and system negotiation, my job trudged on.

My job was supposed to be my stepping stone to the sweet life: making big bucks as an agile coach at an institutional level. subsidiary of a Fortune 100 company wanted an Agile Coach to help deliver better product. Pre-system discovery, I was a well-seasoned scrum master who came with multiple recommendations from many different industry leaders. The interview went well, and, shortly after the start of the pandemic, I accepted the new gig.

It was meant to be my big break; after I had one Agile Coaching job under my belt, the world would be my oyster. And I was good at it! I got moved around from team to team to help them improve, and things were great. I was envisioning a future with cushy, $200k+ jobs in some upscale consulting firm, and the path to that number seemed so, so easy in the pre-system days!

But then, one year into taking my shot at the new place, the system woke up. The future where I dreamed of cushy executive consulting gigs went up in smoke.

Even though I didn’t know it yet, my big-shot tech career began to end in September 2020, when the stress of work finally broke the system free.

My first boss and I got along wonderfully, even going so far as to make us feel at ease discussing our system issues openly. But as the months dragged on, and the chaos inside grew messier, our hold on “the rockstar employee” title began to slip. Even the simplest tasks, like “show up to work on time,” felt like herculean efforts. It started with missing meetings whenever syskids inside chose to throw a fit. Then: forgetfulness, a total lack of motivation, and that all culminated in a complete breakdown of my ability to get work done.

For the first time in my life, I was on the fast track to getting fired.


## Max, 2024

I don’t want to dwell on the sight stuff, but I think it’s important to know that while this was all going on I started losing my vision.

It started in November of 2022.

…that’s a lie. It started way back in 1986, when my family went to see Peter Pan at a drive-in and I couldn’t see a thing. It continued as I went from single vision lenses to bifocals, bifocals to progressives, progressives to low vision specialist gear. So yeah, I’ve known for years that going blind was going to happen with or without my permission.

But November 2022? Yeah. That’s when I couldn’t hide it anymore.

It started with a tough conversation with an optometrist, who said I was no longer able to drive at night. Then it was the retinal specialist, who agreed and referred me to a Lighthouse. (They’re an extension of the Lion’s Club that focuses on the blind and low vision community.) We talked about options for driving and options for navigating the world.

A month later, I had my first white cane and was immediately hooked. I hadn’t realized how much I craved sensory stimulation that my eyes couldn’t bring in, and the more I used the cane, the easier navigating the world got.

(Turns out, I’d been shoulder-checking light poles and tripping over potholes for years. I just forgot when it happened because we’re a system.)

A month after that, I was learning braille and VoiceOver as the text on screen started to blur to the point of uselessness. My world changed seemingly overnight, as I replaced sight-focused skills I built over years with blind-friendly actions.

Nothing changed. I was always a system and I always had low vision. But in 2023, those truths were tossed into a gigantic pressure cooker of stress that eventually exploded into a career crisis that almost ended our life.

Try as we might, we were, quite simply, not up to the task of pretending to be an abled adult human anymore. We tried - boy, did we _ever_ try! - but the ending is still the same.

Welcome to our fuckup arc.

## Potts, 2023

The illustrious Arcades present

A ridealong diary for a professional with DID

Featuring Potts Arcade _(alongside Addie and others, I suppose)_

Names have been changed (and circumstances abridged) to protect the innocent.


### Sam, 7:00AM

My phone played uplifting, quiet music for my wakeup alarm. Not that I needed it in the summer, what with the sun coming up at five and the birds chirping at six, but I wanted some time to myself. I dreamed about getting up, making tea, listening to a record. Really coming to the day slowly, you know? But when I went to leave, my arms reached for Jack-Jack instead.

“No. Stay and snuggle.”

“Hi Lilith.” I forced myself to get up and walk to the bathroom.

“Work’s stupid. Stay in bed. Help the littles feel better.”

Then I was walking _back_ from the bathroom, having done nothing, so that I could pick up Jack-Jack, my newest stuffed animal. Never was a stuffie person before system discovery, but when you have a head full of kids, you end up with lots of stuffies.

As for the bathroom? Sometimes these conversations between, well, _mes_ are smoother when we can talk to each other through a mirror, like a proper crazy person should. The kid refused to let Lilith and I talk without his stuffie, so there I was, holding Jack-Jack’s bunny paw, listening to birdsong, and staring at a blurry face in the mirror that flashed childlike smiles and laughter to me whenever our eyes met.

My face snapped into sudden sternness. Lilith said, “You said the kids could watch Bluey last night.”

“Yeah, the night got away from me.”

Lilith wagged a finger at the mirror. “No, you got selfish. Zoe’s wanted to play that marble game for at _least_ 3 months now. Least you could do is throw on some Bluey for her.”

I looked at the time and pinched the bridge of my nose. Ten minutes before my first meeting, and we were already fighting— 

…wait. ten minutes? How the hell did two hours pass? I waved my fingers in front of my eyes to see if I was dreaming.

No such luck.

“What even happened last night?” I asked the mirror.

A new, calmer countenance washed over my face. Tally. “Addie was out and stole time from the littles.”

“Look, I know. I know. We’ll talk with Addie. This is all so hard to balance.”

“We have to learn.”

“I didn’t even want to be a parent.”

“But we are, and those little ones inside have so, so much patience for your bad parenting.”

Not gonna lie, even if this disorder is some elaborate psychodrama playing in my head, that one hurt.

“When’s the last time you put up the littles’ tent?”

I didn’t answer. She was already aware.

In the real world, I was facilitating a project management meeting. I looked alert, felt present, but my brain was a tangle of angry voices all screaming over one another to be heard.

“Right. Work. I’m getting Addie.”

My vision blurred and the world went dark.

### Addie, 10:03AM

I came to in the middle of my first work meeting. The scent of S’Mores Pop Tart wafted to my nose from a plate nearby. I didn’t remember we had those in the pantry, but I guess there goes my commitment to eating better breakfasts and losing some weight.

The world felt unreal and syrupy. The first two seconds after a switch always start with confusion. Either I can’t tell what day, month, or even year it is; or I can’t remember what I’m doing. There’s even a checklist for such an eventuality:

1. Scan the room for immediate danger.

2. Find a window to determine if it’s day or night.

3. If I’m in front of a computer, determine if I’m on my personal computer or on the work one.

4. Is there someone on a Zoom with me? If so, check the notes in the work folder.

5. Try to not act like a crazy person.

6. Blame your confusion on the weather, the day, or my sleep, whatever works.

My confused face showed on zoom and that spiked my anxiety to 11; did they watch me switch? Do they see it for what it is? Oh gosh, what if they all know I’m crazy and barely beginning to hold things together?

Potts’ voice echoed in my mind. “Relax. They probably see it. Remember we used to, like, assume that people saw every little detail on our face? Like, we never saw it because bad eyes, so we never learned the whole language of facial expressions enough to fake it—“

I did my best to ground. Thought about ice. Ran my finger over a braille stick. Something - anything - to keep me grounded. My head sounded like a coffee shop that had nothing but ticking clocks and anxious voices echoing around brick walls.

During all of this - all of this internal terror and drama and such - _I was still running the meeting._ My voice spoke, my hands danced across the keyboard, but I had no clue who is doing it. Lucy, maybe, based on the voice I was hearing in my head?

“Lucy, darling. Zoe wants some time to journal after your morning meetings. We kind of owe her after last night.”

I sent a thought back at her: “I mean, sure. I wish we’d keep this stuff outside of work hours though.”

Lucy shrugged. “We’re parents now, Addie. And we’re gonna be the best ones we can because they deserve it.”

“I know. I, just… yeah. Disability hard.”

“We know.”

Somehow, my body’s eyes rolled in response to the internal conversation. Shit. Now I’m eye-rolling on camera? Quick: scan the room for any sour faces or angry frowns, just in case I made another mistake.

Pay attention to the stuff people pay you for, Arcades. That’s priority one.

The call ended. My world was now fully dreamlike, and I swear to dog I could pass through walls if I cared a teensy bit less about physics. Through the haze I wrote a quick message to the team in my next meeting informing them that I had to step away for a headache.

Then: open Discord. Open up the right channel for Zoe to write in. Close Teams so that she won’t respond to messages. I took some deep breaths while double-checking the work setup to make sure it was kid-proof.

### Zoe, 10:10AM

(Pasted here from our system chat -Potts)

okay listen

hear me out

poptarts are really good, and s’mores poptarts were the best and I only got them sometimes

but they made me feel happy and safe when I didn’t feel safe at all so uh
