but not the poptarts

and i still want to play something tonight. I understand the marble game is boring so if you want to play video games with me instead that’s okay too. I just want to hang out and do fun things

ok bye

### Addie, 11:00AM

My head pulsed. This always happened when we got switchy. A pounding headache rumbled up from vertigo, amnesia, and confusion.

I ran the switching checklist once again before asking Zoe if I could read her entry. She said it was in the green book, and I knew immediately that that would wait until after work. Potts chimed in: “See? If you give the kids time their needs can be really small. Always give the littles what they need; they _will_ ruin your day to prove a point, if otherwise necessary.”

The next hour passed in a haze. Not a productive haze, either; this was more like one of those “stare into infinite space while your entire body refuses to move, respond, or even feel” kind of hazes. The kind that made Teams message chimes terrifying and every meeting a struggle to stay grounded.

We could hear D coming by their footsteps. They usually came up after their court call was done, mostly to check in and talk about lunch. But something about the way the footsteps fell, and the state we were in, and thinking about the book sent alarm bells ringing. Oh, fuck—

—fudge!

Okay. Hold on a second. There’s a little one with me now while I’m writing this part. Hello little one!. I don’t know who you are but you seem really nice and kind on the inside. I’ll let you pick a face and a name and tell me why you’re here, but first I have to set up some timers, okay?

I can feel my brain falling away from my control, but I’m taking deep, deep breaths to ground. I had to push the kid down for a few more moments, just to wrap up work. I looked to the calendar for my next meeting - a 1:00PM with my boss - and set a timer to wake me up at 12:45.

When that goes off, you have to let me come back, okay? We have adult responsibilities we have to do but I’m so, so glad to hear from you, kiddo!

That’s all the more encouragement it takes for the kid to take control. My eyes roll back into my skull and

### Addie, writing for a new little, 11:30AM

D’s melodic voice echoed through the hallway. “Hey Austen! Done with court! Still want to get lunch with-Oh!”

The second they turned the corner their expression changed. Gone were the daily worries, the troubles, the “adult stuff”; here they were bright and friendly, smiling, child-friendly. 

I spoke without thinking. “Hiiii I’m new and I’m little and I’m a little scared.”

“Nothing to be scared about, little one!” They clapped their hands in that way that makes kids giggle. My body giggled. “Is there anything you want to share?”

“No, I just wanted to be out and Addie said it’s okay for me to be out for lunch so I’m gonna watch some Chip and Dale and play games.”

I did _not_ okay that, mind you. Least, I don’t remember saying it. Things get hazy when you’re trying to work things out on the inside.

I’m… well. I’m trapped. The body is no longer my own, and whatever happens next is completely dependent on the kid driving the body in that moment. Scream, howl, scratch at the amnesia walls in my mind, but that lunch was to be property of this new little.

“Why am I here?”

An even bigger smile crossed D’s face. “You are safe and in 202X. You live in this condo with me, and those bad people can never, ever get to you again!”

“Cool.”

She walks to the window and opens it. The sound of a sea floods into the room along with a caucophony of road noise, ambulance sirens, and the bustling big city.

“Do you wanna see a big lake?”

I walked to the window and stared. Sure, my version of “the lake view” was a chisel-like opening between two buildings, where a smear of greenish-blue lake smeared all the way to the horizon. It wasn’t exactly exciting. But the sound? The force of water pounding the shore, the distant call of ducks raising their family in our little cove? They all sang a wonderful song for me that more than made up for my lack of vision.

“You never had a lake like this groing up, eh?”

“We went out to Raccoon Lake,” the kid said. “Back when dad bought a boat.”

“Well, this lake goes on past the horizon! It’s huge!” They gave my hair a tussle. “Do you want anything to eat? I’m sure you’re hungry because I’m hungry too!”

“Uh, yeah.” My voice is thin, reedy. _Scared. She’ll tell the others we’re here and then the guys will show up and_

“I’ll make anything you want.”

“Mac and cheese, please!”

“Okay!” They grabbed a green book from the shelf and handed it to me. “You hang tight and draw in your journal, and I’ll be right back!”

So I got some crayons and drew and drew and felt warm and safe until D returned with a kraft dinner with hotdogs cut into it. “Here you go, kiddo!”

“It’s okay if I eat in bed?”

“You can eat wherever you want! Nobody’s here to tell you otherwise!” They leaned in. “Would you like a forehead kiss, kiddo?”

“Yeah!”

They gave us the gentlest forehead kiss. They always give good kisses. “Fankoo for getting this for me.”

“Of course,” my wife replied. “Let me know if you need anything, kiddo!”


### Twi, 1:15PM

“I’m still not seeing the consistent progress I want to see.”

The voice was familiar but hazy. Authority? Boss. Boss! 

Last time I was out, it was noon on a Saturday. But here I was, sitting on a zoom call with no idea what was going on. Looking down, I saw a half-eaten bowl of mac and cheese, and an muted tablet playing Chip and Dale.

_The fuck is going on?_ I thought.

I scrambled through notes, looking for my list of “things to brag about.” There had to be something that was demonstrably better now than it was a few weeks ago, but honestly? I knew the decision was already made. I was a dead enby walking. I didn’t want to admit it yet but I knew the pattern because I’d been on the other side of the management fence before.

This was a mid-year performance review, triggered by an ADA process from my vision, and the verdict is in: I wasn’t improving enough to make the cut.

“I’m really sorry to drop this on you before a big vacation, but I wanted you to know what came out of the accommodation process. You’re still not where I need you to be.”

Inside, my body was a scramble of emotions. Terror. Fear of physical harm, if you believe it. Nausea. Vertigo. You name it, I was experiencing it. Outside, my eyes unfocused and my mouth fell slightly agape and _I want to let the world go fuzzy so the emotions stop hurting me—_

“I hope you have a great vacation, though!”

She was genuine. The news sucks but she was doing the best she can, so I smiled and waved and ended the call. Inside, I was a pile of crabs, all trying to climb to the top of the bucket to peer out on what just happened.


### Addie, 4:00PM

I came to without knowing where the day went.

There’s a beer by the work computer and I realize, to my horror, that I’m drinking during work hours. (Gotta love a good wagon fall on the company dime, I guess.) But honestly, nothing I did or say was going to make things better at work. Eseentially, my day ended before it started because I didn’t listen to the littles.

All around my office were half-finished tasks: drying loads of laundry, half-built lego structures, and the dulcet tones of a mascot professional wrestling league blaring from my computer speakers. Atop the work laptop was a green book with a sticky note: “NOT UNTIL AFTER WORK.”

Not that I needed a warning with the green book. I knew better.

This is how you survive as a mentally disabled person: you get long strings of being unable to focus at work, and then you hope for the 10x, 20x days where you have plenty of spoons to spend. When it happens, you push the accelerator to the floor, hoping to whoever will listen that the effort will get you a few weeks ahead before the bad times returned.

Like Twi said, once the board broke there was going to be a mighty mess to clean up.

I didn’t message any coworkers. Didn’t say goodbye. I just went downstairs, packed a bowl, and took a long, rough drag. Inside I could feel the others beating at the surface of my consciousness, demanding to have their say.

We were about to ride the rolodex.

For those who haven’t experienced it before, the rolodex is a state we wouldn’t wish on our worst enemy. For the next hour, we were going to rapidly cycle through alters, sometimes with as little as seconds between switches. Each switch brings with it complete amnesia, more dissociation, and confusion.

Essentially, picture giving an old rolodex a quick spin so it cycles through all your contacts, but it’s inside your brain.

Every single time the switch happened, we’d look to D and say “Hi, I am not well.” And they would give us a hug and explain, yet again, that the system wrote in the green book today.

I’m not well, indeed. This system stuff is for the fucking birds.


### Potts, 10:00PM

“I think I finally have control.”

I was on the couch with my wife. My eyes were moist - pillow, too, and my body was twisted to both be as fetal and as close to D as possible. A breath later, my shoulders relaxed.

“It’s an adult on this end,” I said.

“Mostly,” D said with a laugh.

“Oh hush. I’m grown now.” They were referring to last year, when I acted like a 9 year old boy. Lots of poop jokes, of course, care of our main subscriber Butthole Spas, where your number two is our number one priority. (You’re welcome, D!) But since that time, I’ve become sort of a system dad.

“Bad night?”

I shrugged. “I guess, yeah. Not like I can remember it.”

“Yeah.”

We were silent for a moment. On the coffee table was the green book from earlier, and inside were drawings of a naked child being held at knifepoint.

“I just… it’s hard to believe, D. Fucking terrifying.”

Their voice, calm and measured, relaxed me. “I’ve met the kids, hon. This is all real.”

“I just… yeah.” Addie shaded in to talk for a moment. “I’m just afraid of being this disabled. I feel like this is my last chance to have a career before I’m too disabled to get a job.”

“That’s not true.”

“I know. I just feel it.”  I tried to chuckle, but switching Addie back out was taking my all my concentration. “Fuck. I’m hungry. Did we get too high?”

“I didn’t pay attention.” This was D-speak for “You probably had more than you think because of your amnesia.” When I ask inside nobody can really tell me how much I’ve had.

“It’s 10, right? Did we eat dinner?”

“Yes.”

“Fuck it. I’ve got the munchies and am ordering delivery. If I’m gonna have to go out tonight with a flashback-brain I’m at least going out the way I want to: full of delicious steamed trash.” 

Steamed trash, in this case, represents of the greatest gifts the Midwest has ever given the world. Thousands have made pilgrimages here just to sample its heavenly, oniony aroma. It’s grandeur can be seen on the silver screens of the world, but truly, to know this, the True Face of God Arisen as Fast Food, you must journey to the middle of nowhere, USA and sidle up for a 10-sack.

I was talking, of course, about a big ass bag of White Castle sliders.

D laughed. “I’m out. How in the hell are you going to hit delivery?”

I raised an eyebrow. “Ordering enough to _get_ delivery is just one part of the challenge.”


### Addie, 8:00AM

I woke with a shot. Outside, birds sang and the tide washed in fresh seaglass from Lady Michigan. Inside, I felt like my stomach was demanding to speak to the manager.

“You should look at your phone,” my wife told me.

I stood - mostly because otherwise I’d puke - and made my way downstairs while looking at the photos presented for me. There, the remains of a feast of ten sliders, fries, and at least one mozzarella stick covered the coffee table.

The photos showed a veritable burger bouquet, with multiple types of sliders and a mini-buffet of different dips to try. The bastard had even pulled out our silly turkey serving plate to lay the burgers out all fancy-like, and he added balsamic drizzle for garnish.

_A balsamic drizzle._

“What day is it, D?”

“Saturday.”

I breathed deep - not too deep - and heaved a belch.

“What the fuck happened yesterday?” I asked, and D just laughed.

# 7

# Wig in a Box

> > Suddenly I’m this punk rock
> > star of stage and screen
> > And I ain’t ever—
> > I’m never going back!
> > 
## Twi, 2024

One of the most annoying symptoms of DID is also one of the more bizarre: a person with DID will rabidly deny they have the disorder.

The system will twist itself into mental pretzels trying to doubt the existence of alters within the body. We spent the better part of a year trying to argue ourselves out of being a system, each of us choosing different arguments to make the case that this is something else - schizophrenia, bipolar, *anything* but DID.

Because the system worked best when it was hidden. It kept us alive when the worst things happened because nobody knew we were a lot of people in a single head. The brain’s not exactly pining to let go of its defense mechanism so easily. So each of us would step up to the plate, make our case to the other alters in the system on why we couldn’t possibly exist as a “we,” and then the alters would fight it out in hours-long conversations that dragged on into the night.

Again, these conversations were about how we *couldn’t possibly have alters.* This disorder, man.


## Addie, 2008

“Goddamn it, I told you all that I wasn’t writing a trans memoir.”

The words echoed around the arcade inside. Not one single alter responded, of course. None of us particularly enjoyed being back in the aughts. It was a rough time and we put in so, so much work to delete the memory from our history. But I guess Twi wants to touch on it, so I’m stuck writing about it.

The year 2009 was, without a doubt, the worst year of my life. The cruelty of this year transformed me body and soul. My old life fell completely apart, and from its ashes I built who I am today.

…And I guess it’s the first year I met Tally, so that’s worth a chapter at least.


## Zoe, 1988

I found out the difference between boys and girls when I was four years old and I didn’t have a word big enough to describe how much I hated being on the wrong side. I tried trepidatious (my mom likes it) but that’s not quite it. Dead? No. I wasn’t really dead so that didn’t work either. But… uh, what’s the feeling you get when you get in trouble but it’s not your fault and you know you’re gonna have to take someone else’s punishment?

Well, like that times a thousand for little me.

The first day of preschool I got to have recess with all my new friends. It was the 80s then: the playground was full of metal jungle gyms, a small dirt pit with a basketball hoop, and some toys piled near the shed which housed them. I tried to join the girls on the swings. I didn’t know why but i liked them better and I didn’t want to play with the gross boys in the basketball dirt pit. It was so messy there!

I sat at a swing and waited for the girls to say hi.

“What are you doing?” one girl said. She had curly blonde hair, I remembered that most of all. This little girl with curly, blonde hair sneering at boy-me.

“I wanna play,” I said. I smiled. People like when you smile at them. Mom taught me that. Maybe it would be enough to earn their friendship—?

“You’re a boy,” she snapped, pointing to the mud pit where boys were currently roughhousing over a ball. I didn’t want to go over there, but I didn’t want to be alone, so I swallowed the lump in my throat and tried my best to blend in with the boys.

When I came back inside from recess I was filthy dirty and super sad, but I didn’t cry. Dad would be proud.

But I had a secret weapon for this kind of sadness: I can just forget it.

I was four and I know the best magic trick ever. I could just stop crying or being excited or anything if I tried hard enough. It was like, uh, like popping my knuckles but in my brain and when I do it I feel better. It helps when people yell at me.

So I thought really hard about it - like, really really hard! - until my brain popped. I didn’t care about the girls anymore, or that my clothes were filthy. It was easier when I tried to be what other people wanted anyways because then people didn’t figure out how weird I was.

I figured out how to be a good boy. Eventually. Mom and Dad would be proud.


## Tally, 2008

This wasn’t the first time Addie and I met. For that, you have to go back to a furry con in Michigan, where I dressed up as a girl, picked up a guy at a furry con, and ruined a lot of my friends nights in the process. I promise I can explain.

The con itself was a bit of a mess. It was in one of those old Holiday Inn resort hotels, where there's a pool and a hot tub and a sauna and a bunch of open space for loosely organized activities. Years after the fact we would discover that the con organizer was a nazi, but at the time we were having fun with friends at a tiny con. There was always free beer on tap, and as a freshly-minted, post-college adult I got into the sauce quite a bit.

But the drunker I got, the less I could hear the others and the more I could feel my heart buzzing with thoughts of Brandon.

My coming out at the con came in fits and starts from there. I told everyone that I'm a girl, at one point. Or at least that I wasn’t a boy. And after throwing on a skirt and top that I brought for the occasion I convinced everyone to go to the dance with Brandon.

It's a mess of a dance. There are lasers, smoke machines, house music, and folks in fursuits swaying to the music all around us. But I was dancing with him, and honestly? And in that dance, all I could see is Brandon.

He had the most beautiful eyes. Clean shaven. And there's this wonderful spot under his strong chin where I could nock my head into the small of his chest and sway with him. We're enveloped in green lasers and fog that smelled like lemon pledge and when he throws his arms around my tunic I just feel like... like me. Completely and totally me, in a way that had never happened before.

We tumbled to the floor together. he straddled me. (Look. Furry cons in the aughts were weird.) Our foreheads touched; his fingers caressed my check like a feather. I was… I know that there, in that moment, I'm his girl. And nobody in my head can tell me otherwise.

"I want you," he said. His gentle smile filled my head with a pink haze, impossible to think through. I felt pressure at the back of my head and tried my best to tamp it back down.

His bright eyes shone like galaxies, his soft smile filled me with gentle confidence. It was then, or never again. Even if I hurt people that day, you have to understand. I thought this was the one and only chance I’d ever have to be seen as, well, _me_.

"I want to sleep with him," I told my girlfriend. Her face scrunched up with disgust. I know that Potts would have read between the scrunched-up lines of her shocked face, that I should have retracted the request, give Brandon a hug, and gone back to the hotel room with her. We would have laid in bed. Cuddled. Cried. I would have apologized for letting myself be a girl in front of everyone.

But it’s not who I want to be in that moment, so I promptly ignore the warning signs. “Please,”

"If it will get this out of your system," she says, lingering on system. Her fingers curl around mine with a weak grip, but I had what I wanted out of the conversation. I turned to Brandon with a smile that sparkled like a thousand suns.

I was his girl that night.

I was a girl.


## Potts, 2008

I woke up that morning in a my friend Brandon's bed, naked, his taste on my tongue. Panic set in. I got out of the bed, knowing I did something wrong, but not exactly knowing why. My phone had dozens and dozens of missed calls, missed texts, and pleas for me to pick up. Nobody else in my friend group slept that night; they stayed up in the laundry room comforting our girlfriend. *My* girlfriend.
 
Why couldn’t I remember last night? Why did I sleep with this guy if I don’t even like men?
 
"Everything okay?" Brandon asks.
 
I gathered my clothes. 
 
"No."
 
He chuckled. "First time with a guy? I can smell the shame from here."
 
"No. Been with men before, no hangups there. Just... that was reckless."
 
He smiled at me. "You were beautiful last night, dear."
 
"Dear?"
 
The memories come flooding back. The skirt. The dancing. The… way I let him take care of me, like his girl. His *girl*.
 
"You got your girlfriend’s permission. I heard it."
 
"I know. I'm sorry. I don't know what came over me."
 
"It's fine."
 
I scrolled through the messages on my phone. “Gods, these texts. I ruined everything. This is their *hotel room*, and I locked them out all night to sleep with you. They're in the laundry room right now. Have been all night. God *damnit*, I'm not this asshole."
 
Brandon gathered the sheets around him as he sat up. "Look, it's an innocent mistake. And they could have come in at any time - we could always have gotten dressed."
 
The texts started up again. "I have to go," I say.
 
He looked to the bed, defeated. I put a hand on his shoulder. "This wasn't your fault. It's my fault for getting you into this."

“Thanks.” I left him to pick up his clothes at his own pace.

*It's always my fault.* I repeated as I ran to the laundry room. I found my girlfriend there, staring out the window stern look on her face. I was about to get the shit scolded out of me.
 
"Why did you do it?" she asked.

I pleaded with her with my eyes. "I don't know."
 
"You're serious."
 
"Yes," I say. "I just... I don't know what came over me. I'm not a girl. I'm not into men. and I'm in love with you." Two truths, one lie, but at that point I'd say whatever it took to make this conversation end. Not knowing how it would end was making me feel queasy and deeply uncomfortable.
 
I couldn’t remember any of it.

"I can't believe you'd do this to me."
 
"I can't either."

We dropped it and tried to enjoy our last night at the con, but I had to teach high school the next day and proceeded to have a full-on panic attack about it in front of all my friends.

Misty got tail. I got panic attacks, a drama bomb in my friend group, and generally given the short end of the stick. Being plural is a bitch, sometimes.


## Sam, 2006

I got ma’amed for the first time at a Halloween frat party.

To start with, we went to an all-male college. (We got better, though!) And every Halloween since our freshman year we made a point to dress up as a girl for a costume. The queer community where I live used to call Halloween “Trans Christmas”, as it gave us genderweird folk an ironclad excuse to give new gender presentations a try. Either you got a laugh out of your friends, or you got genuine compliments about how nice you look as a girl.

I remembered the dress so clearly: it was a salvage shop find from Lafayette, IN, where I would go out dressed to avoid getting clocked by anyone I knew. It was this red, yellow, and orange floral print sundress, and I paired it with black mary-janes to pull the look together.

(This was before the time of cell phone cameras, so nobody has photo evidence of this moment actually happening. Which is fantastic, because early transition pictures make me cringe.)

I was also zooted; a friend had just offered a gravity bong hit and whatever was in the pipe had me moon-rocks high. Everyone else said getting high chilled their brain out but for me, I could start talking to other people in my head. It was really weird, but the others are pretty cool with meeting up here and there for a party, long as I forgot about talking to them afterwards.

A friend - we’ll call him Matt - called me over to the beer pong table and I obliged him. We were halfway through a game when I felt a tap at my shoulder.

“Where’s your costume?” he said. When I turned I realized it was another friend - we’ll call him Chad - who legit did not recognize me in a dress. Usually I’d have told him straight away but I had a sudden impulse in the moment.

What if I just let him think I’m a girl?

I tried the voice one of the trans girls at the Blind Pig taught me. “I’m here with a friend.”

“A friend?” His lips curled. “Not a boyfriend, I hope.”

The words lit a blazing fire in my heart. I’d been seen before. Adored even. Victory parties? I’ve had em. Girlfriend? Yep, right there at the corner of the room, sulking while I wore the biggest smile. There should have been nothing different about this attention, but why was my heart pounding a million times a minute and my stomach covered in butterflies?

I was being pursued. Like a real girl.

“You’re cute,” he said. My heart took flight. Not for Chad, per se. But that, for the first time, someone _saw_ me.

It was around that time Matt checked in on the conversation and blew it for me. “Oh hey, buddy. That’s Austen in the dress!”

The spell broken, he pushed away from me. I tried to play it off as a joke. “Thanks for the kind words, sugar,” I said, giving a curtsey for good measure. We laughed it off as a joke in the moment, but that blossoming, sweet feeling stuck with me for months. Being seen, pursued, adored.

I was hooked.


## Samantha, 2007

The adults wanted to show our girlfriend how to play disc golf but I wanted to lay down in the grass so there we were, in the fairway of the disc golf course, staring at blades of grass and trying to keep the lid on the big secret.

The big secret would kill us. We know that because our family didn’t like gay people and well, they made us act manly. But laying on that grass, the blades tickling my arms, I just wanted the pain of living in the wrong body to be over.

I wanted to stop hiding.

I wanted to be a girl.

Yeah I know we were in college but I hated everything about my body and if I had my vote I’d have just started transition when we went to college. But Addie wanted to finish school first, which was dumb but at least she lets me get dressed on the weekends here and there as long as I did it in Lafayette where nobody can see me.

I stared at the grass and yelled at the other voices to go away. That helped sometimes - if I got really sad, or really upset, they’d just go away because they were scared of the emotions. So I imagined going home to see dad and that got everyone to run away real quick.

“Are you okay?” my girlfriend asked. Well, she’s the system’s girlfriend, not my girlfriend. I was too young for that. She’s uh, well, we call our wife “big friend” when this happens so I guess the girlfriend was a big friend too.

“I want to be a girl.”

“Are you sure?”

“More than anything.”

“Then let’s get started,” she said, and I felt the system rumble and roar with rage. The world shrank to a pinpoint, popped out of existence, and I was back in the dark place while the adults undid everything I had done.


## Addie, 2009

I was twenty-five years old and making twelve dollars an hour without health insurance. Of course I didn’t go to a therapist when I started hearing the voices. Instead, I tried to talk them down on my own.

We were in a nondescript parking garage on the north side of Indianapolis walking home from the call center. We worked for a skeezy life insurance company whose sales model looked very, shall we say, pyramid-shaped from my perspective as this low level contracted tech. But they paid, and the job wasn’t too hard, so there we stayed.

At that point in my history I was a regimented, terrified mess of a human being. Every morning at 10 I’d wake up, dress like a guy, and apply to jobs. Sometimes at home, sometimes at whatever job fair was going at the time hoping to be seen. Every night and weekend I’d dress up in comfortable women’s clothes and marvel at how my body was changing.

When I wasn’t doing that I was either drunk or talking to the people in my head and trying to make sense of the what was happening.

Back in the parking garage I was talking to myself: “So if I were to think of a number–”

“It doesn’t work that way,” Tally said. “I have no clue what number you’re thinking of. That’s the whole point of amnesia barriers.”

“So we can hold a conversation, and I don’t have to dream up what you’re saying. It just pops into my head.”

She slid into our car and pulled the mirror so that we could look at our own reflection: the closest thing to a face-to-face meeting we could muster. “We are two separate people, Addie. Of course you can’t fill in the blanks for me.”

“Am I like, possessed or something?”

“Maybe,” Tally made the body shrug. “Look, I’m not a theologist or anything. Just a voice in your head that wants to help.”

I wanted to fight her on this, but I know better. When you’ve gone from white collar professional with career aspirations to hourly tech support, there’s sort of nowhere to go but up. I was laid low and Tally knew it.

She finished the thought for me. “I’ve been waiting for you to hit rock bottom, yes. It’s the only ay you’d believe me.”

“That’s not true.”

She started the car. “Girl, I live in this body with you. Don’t lie.”

“So what do we do now?“

“New job, to start with. We keep hustling and hoping.”

“I’m tired, though.”

“We can be tired once we have a good paying job,” Tally responded, using my mouth, my lips, my voice to chide me. I can’t emphasize enough how strange and puppet-like I felt when the body starts moving on its own.

“Maybe I can talk to mom and dad,” I offered. “Try to smooth things over, get some networking help–”

“Fuck them,” Tally said. “I know you can’t remember why, but fuck them. We’re better off leaving them in our dust.”


## Misty, 2001
I hid a bag of girl’s clothes in my car trunk that would only come out in my basement room at mom’s.

I didn’t bother with it at dad’s because it was far, far too dangerous to try. But here at mom’s, it got eerily quiet at night and everyone slept super sound. If I kept the lights low and didn’t make much noise I could try things on and see how I felt.

I was seventeen years old and trying on girl clothing for the first time.

The look was a disaster, of course. I paired an off-the-shoulder, dingy heather top with a maroon, salvage-shop skater skirt. But it was the closest I’d ever come to building a full outfit instead of stealing my sister’s clothes, and I wanted to see what it would look like. So I slung a borrowed bra onto my chest, the shirt, the skirt, and just as the look was impossible to take off quickly I heard a flush of the toilet above me. 

I laid in bed for a half hour with the lights out, wrapped tightly in the covers, terrified that if someone heard me while using the bathroom they’d check in on me. Then I stood - slowly, quietly! - from the bed and flipped on the lights next to the mirror.

I was a mess.

Let me make my point clear: the word “travesty” doesn’t even begin to cover this look, and that’s even keeping in mind that travesty is actually based on a term used for trans women in France. It was ugly, hung weird off my well-muscled body, and I wouldn’t have been found dead outside of my room in this getup.

But the potential was _there_.

I stuffed the bra and threw on a snapback to cover up my haircut. The figure in the mirror softened as I spun the skirt to hide my lack of hips. Then, in the blink of an eye, I saw myself, visibly female.

I felt as if someone had dropped a rock in the still pond that was my mind, and those ripples picked up speed until they were full-on tidal waves pounding on the edges of my heart.

“My body is _wrong_.“ I said it to the the reflection in the mirror. ”I’m a girl.“

Then: ”I can’t let anybody know about this.“

The next morning, while taking out the trash, I slipped the skirt and shirt into the garbage while no one was looking and popped my brain so I didn’t get hurt missing the outfit.

Turns out being able to voluntarily forget things can come in handy, sometimes.



## Misty, 2008

I got my first shot of estrogen in a clandestine clinic in Indianapolis. It took up the second floor of a greystone just north of downtown, placed in a residential district. The sign was hidden, too; the only way in was to know someone who knew someone. (Protests would have shut that place down if they knew about it.) But there I was, looking out the window and trying to calm the terror in my heart, looking at a needle full of yellowish oil that hovered above my thigh.

I knew that starting hormones would kick off a shitstorm at home. I knew that I’d lose my entire family for daring to do this. But I had to. There was no choice; only gravity invariably leading to the conclusion that I was a woman.

Estelle, the nurse, calmed me with a hand on the knee. “It’s just a shot,” she offered. “You’re going to be okay.”

“I know.” I looked outside. What would mom do when she finds out I did this behind her back? Sure, I was 23 and she can’t do anything about it, but she always got angry when I do something without running it by her first. Maybe I could refuse the shot and run it by her, just in case–

Estelle almost had to snap her fingers to get my attention, but when she did the took my hand into hers. “This moment is for you. Not for anybody else. Do you still want this?”

I nodded.

“Let’s do it,” I said, and Momma Estelle plunged the needle that started this whole journey.

In time, Estelle would become known as my Mother, the kind of setup that you’d see in 80s drag balls. Her and Aunt Vicky and Aunt Asia would spend the next few years making sure I could function in the world as a woman, offering makeup tips, hand-me-downs, and a shoulder to cry on when needed.

In that moment, though, she was another villager helping to raise this idiot. And honestly, this is all I ever wanted when I came out as trans. Not to defend myself, not to haggle over how much someone will accept the new me. I just needed someone to hold my hand and tell me it was okay.

When I left the office my heart felt like it could burst with joy. I turned on the loudest, girliest summer pop I could and sang at the top of my lungs for fear that holding in this emotion would actually kill me. And to this day, I’m still angry that this amazing moment, one of the happiest of my life, had to be experienced alone because I didn’t trust my family enough to be a part of the experience.


## Lilith, 2024

It was never about respect with my parents. I’m still not even sure what they wanted out of me.

All I ever wanted out of them was an "I love you" or an "I support you." That's _it_. Instead, I had to claw every little bit of interaction out of them because me being queer was icky to them, and that I had been angry when I initially came out fourteen years ago.

I could dress the facts up all I wanted but the fact remains bare: "you are trans, and we hate that about you."

It's not who you eventually become that proves your love. I’ve seen my fair share of people be transphobic and eventually come around to be girls themselves. (I’m mostly joking. Mostly.) People change, and we all become better along the way. That’s normal.

Maybe they chose rejection out of fear. Maybe their religion was against it. Maybe they worried what the town would think. But in that moment, in that split second between listening to their child bear their heart and opening their mouth, they chose rejection

Once you’ve been rejected like that, there is no reconciliation. And for folks like us, the second you show that hand, we can no longer trust you. You become dangerous. Radioactive, even, to my chances of survival. Either we can trust someone entirely or we can’t trust them at all, and our parents proved over and over again that they were not to be trusted.

The others may disagree about airing this part out loud but you know what? Good fucking riddance to my parents. My heart is lighter and brighter for letting them go.


## Addie, 2008

We spent our first Christmas alone at the age of 24. I’d recently lost my job, my girlfriend, and my stable living situation, all in a matter of weeks. I’d scraped up enough to lease an apartment in a crimey part of town, and if I didn’t find a job in the next fourteen days I wouldn’t be able to make rent.

I also had my tonsils out a week ago, so that’s fun.

I was about three months into hormone replacement therapy, and I wasn’t sure if the constant crying is my hormones being off or things being just that bad.

We didn’t remember much about the day other than that we were drunk on cheap vodka, full of Kraft Dinner, and stalking Facebook, flipping through photos of my mother’s third wedding.

I had not been invited this time around. Reason: I’m a girl now. I think she said something to the tune of “I can’t bear to hear your voice or see your face.” Christmas was cancelled too, and I felt afraid of showing to my dad’s family’s thing for some unknown reason, so there I was. Alone on Christmas, with a belly full of hyperprocessed mac and cheese, crying over all the people in those pictures that I could never see again.

To this day, I do not celebrate Christmas because of this moment. No joke. Over the course of the last three months I’d been ridiculed, rejected, insulted, and generally treated as a pariah and a bother by my family. A switch flipped in me, and for the first time in my life I learned how to say no.

“No mother, I won’t go to another therapist to prove this to you.”

“No, I’m not stopping this treatment.”

“No. I don’t care what the town thinks.”

“No, it’s not negotiable.”

And then, from her: “No, you’re not welcome here anymore.”

I heard a voice echoing from the bedroom of my apartment. “Was it worth it?”

“You bet your ass,” I replied.

To nobody.

In a 1 bedroom apartment I didn’t share.

“Wait, what the fuck was that?”


## Tally, 2009

For the first time, Addie and I met each other. Like, really truly saw each other. It’s hard to explain with words, but most times, when you’re a system you have big mood swings and don’t know how to react to them, right? But you can’t remember being emotional because that’s dangerous, so you forget that you’re sad or mad or whatever, and poof! The emotion’s just gone.

Problem was, we didn’t know where all the wayward emotions go. Turns out, you can only stuff emotions down so far before things start breaking. Your brain is very, very capable of forgetting, but the garbage is still there, festering in some far-off corner of your brain.

I walked the mirror to look at myself. I still had the masculine jaw line, still had stubble, and still felt like I was making a huge mistake.
 
And because of my gender I was spending Christmas alone. Or at least I thought so.  

"That's my face?"  
  
We weren't sure who said it but when we got to the mirror the world felt unreal and strange.

"Who are you?” Addie asked aloud.

My face scrunched for a moment. I yawned. The world went fuzzy, and then I was back in control of the body.

"I'm Tally. I'm here to help."

"Help with what?"

"Confidence." My lips curled into a gentle smile. "You made a good decision, starting transition. You _feel_ better. Now I want us to live like it."

"Okay, so I'm officially crazy."

"As a loon. But there's good news too: you are a girl! And you get to meet me!"

I laughed. What else are you supposed to do when the reflection in the mirror talks back to you? I thought about trying to sleep, or recalling the day to make sure I wasn't drunk or something, but my body was no longer my own. I felt like a puppet on strings, controlled by an invisible hand that was at once my own body and a complete stranger driving at the wheel.

So yeah. I started transition, lost my family, and nearly lost my apartment at 23, 2 months into the Great Recession. How'd that go? Let's just leave it at "we didn't handle it well."


## Misty, 2021

Mom would make a point to reach out a few times over the years that followed.

Like the time during the pandemic when we had a relationship for about six months. She started calling because her father was dying, and I guess that got her to thinking about me?

And me, being the sucker I am, I believed her at her word: it was just talking, after all. Nothing bad could come from that.

First red flag I should have seen was that there was never reconciliation. Or even an apology. Just "how's life," like I'd been off on a European vacation for the past few years. We must never talk about the conflict directly, after all!

"I just wish you hadn't been so harsh at the start," she said. It has been 14 years since 2008 and we're still talking about three months in that year like it's the worst thing I've ever done.

"I was in a rough spot," I said, shrugging. Looking back always got me apologetic. There was always something to look back on and apologize for, some thing I did wrong that I still couldn't atone for. But at least I had a mom again. “You abandoned me.”

“You were too harsh,” she replied.

I even _visited Indiana_ at this time, and she had the gall to pretend the past didn't exist at all. That she was always an ally, that she had never thrown me out into the cold, that she and I had been buds for a long, long time. It was exhausting!

I told her that I had DID. Her response: "Oh, okay."

Thing was, it wasn't about the disagreement. Not about me being a trans woman. It wasn't even about the abuse! It was how I had embarrassed her in front of the town, how I had grown past her.

Her favorite response was silence. Oh, my mom was a master of the silent treatment! Never discussed, never directly said, she'd send family members to try and talk me out of transition, sometimes. She'd freeze you out of a family for saying a single bad word of her, I swear to you. And Lord help you if you are queer and want to be in her good graces!

Fast forward to November 2021. It was my birthday and we’d just recently rediscovered the system. We were a mess, but at least we're trying to heal. I was still heady from the whole "my mom loves me again" arc of my relationship with mom, so when my birthday came around all I wanted was a phone call and a check-in.

I waited. Nothing.

I called. Answering machine.

I texted: "Are you okay? Just wanted to check in."

An hour went by. I tried not to think about it but the silence was starting to eat at me. Was she okay? Maybe I'd said something to upset her again. Maybe she was in hospital for another surgery - she had a lot of surgeries when I was growing up.

Three hours later I get a response: "This is your sister. Mom isn't talking to you because you told someone in town you weren't talking to her, and that hurt her feelings. She'll reach out when she's ready."

I'm not too proud to say this made me fall out. Hard. Turns out giving a system the silent treatment is a guaranteed way to get the kids in the system whipped into a crying spree. Of _course_ I’d screwed this up again. Of _course_ I'd say something to push her away again; that's all I'm good at!

The thoughts swirled in my head as D and I considered our options over champagne and somber music.

"I'm so sorry," D said. "I had a feeling this would happen sooner or later."

"I know. I just wish—“

Silence.

"What?"

"I wish I could do something," I said, smiling. "And actually, I think we can."

I pulled out my phone and opened up our system chat. It was an app grafted onto a platform called Discord that allowed me to "converse" with proxied avatars of my alters. Other systems turned me on to it and we'd been living with the system server as a constant companion for months at that point. We ran every decision through it, big or small, so that everyone inside could have equal say in decisions that affected our life.

I typed out a message:

"What do we do about mom?"

My fingers sped across the keys without my prompting.

"Dump her."

"She's fucked around for the last time. Time she finds out."

"She's a meanie!"

I looked to D. “I… I think we're in agreement on this one. That's weird."

"Weird?"

"For years, when she'd do this I'd shut down. Felt like I made a mistake, and that I had to make it right. I'd write her letters, or sit with her while she sulked. Something, anything to make things better."

"What changed?"

"For the first time in a long time? We know she's in the wrong."

"Yeah?"

"And we can vote on taking our next action," I finished with a smile. “We’re done with her.


## Tally, 2008

All our life we've made ourselves small to avoid being seen.

First it was to avoid being bullied at school. I’d watch my own body language like a hawk to make sure I didn't make myself a target. Then at home I kept busy with quiet, lonely activities to keep from exposing too much of my personal self to people who could hurt us. Stuff like painting, or listening to music, or writing.

Oh, writing! We loved to write precisely because many folks in our family hated reading. I could write thinly-veiled pieces of fiction that told people the truth about how I was being treated, and nobody was the wiser. Once I started getting older I pushed harder into the writing; choosing to skip dinners out, movies, other activities to have the most serene moments of calm and clarity that came alongside an empty, quiet home.

I spent my entire high school career hiding and my college career trying to peek out from under the fear. But transition? That wasn’t something you can do "small." Certainly not in this year of our Lord 2008! After five years of an abusive relationship and a lifetime of hiding myself for fear of reprisal, I was mighty tired of the dance by the time my mom invited me over to talk about "the trans thing." And of course, I said yes.

In the five years between my coming out and this conversation we’d barely been in contact. I kept up with monthly emails updating her on how life is been. In return, I got emails about how she "couldn't bear to see my face" and that my "voice makes her cry." But suddenly she's sweet as honey, all smiles and nostalgia as we went through the kayfabe of a normal family dinner. Long as you ignore the tension you could squint your eyes and see a normal conversation.

At least I get to see my dog again. Silver linings have been worse before.

Addie didn’t attend this meeting. She has a bad habit of, well, rolling over for our family, for lack of a better word. This wasn’t a day for surrender. So as we sat in the living room, rum and cokes in hand, I poked the elephant in the room:

"Okay. You dropped me like a bad habit for five years. What changed?"

"Oh, it's not that big a thing!" My mom has that tone to her voice. I don't have a good word for it, but it feels cloying, desperate. Weak, in some ways, but the message was delivered with confidence. "I just wanted to check in with you."

"That's not why we are here," I said. There was silence. I sipped at my drink. My dog did her level-headed best to merge with my lap; after so many years apart I was going to have to pry this dog off with a crowbar.

“Stop. Tell me what you want from me, or I walk."

Addie would _never_ say that. It’s why this fight had to be mine; I had the spine for it.

"You don't have to be touchy about it!" she said, half joking. My stern face told her otherwise. (Somewhere deep inside I felt anger, hatred, and rage boiling up -- food for thought later, after I was out of the house. For now, focus on the prize. Nail down her ask.)

"I heard you're going to Thailand for surgery."

It’s true; in a month I’d be boarding a plane to Thailand to visit Dr. Suporn for bottom surgery. I had already paid a five-figure deposit to secure my surgery date.

Of course. The fight was always about my gender.

"He's the best in the world," I said with a shrug. "Wasn't going to trust it to anyone else. I fly out next week and am very excited--"

"It seems so dangerous," she said. “Maybe you should get a second opinion.”

“Mom, I’m already in for twelve thousand dollars. You’re a little late to stop me.”

“Still, what if something happens?”

I should have been surprised. It would have been so much easier if it were surprise; I could have been impressed by the sheer balls of my mother ignoring me for five years only to reach out and try and ask me to stop my transition. Like I owed her a favor or something. I couldn’t help laughing.

"Oh my god, mom."

"Hear me out!"

"You have no right to ask that."

"It just seems dangerous. Maybe you and I could, I don't know, sit together and figure this out?"

I was full-on laughing at that point. What else do you do when someone's suckered you in with a homecooked meal only to sell you on some pipe-dream of a reality where I was not trans and things went back to the place where I was miserable and small, but loved by the family?

"That's what this was about? Butter me up so you can talk me out of the surgery?"

"I just don't think you've given this enough thought--"

“You're too late. And furthermore, you're _way_ out of line, pretending you have enough of my respect to ask me to throw my dream away. How dare you!”

"I never said that."

"You did. 'What will the neighbors think?', I believe you said. February. 2009.” I pointed to the kitchen table. “We sat right there when I came out as trans and you said those words. I’ll never forget them."

The smile gave way to a sneer. It always gave way to the sneer, once you said no to whatever she asked. That's just how mom was. And here, cornered and now exposed, she went to the only thing she knew: blame. "You didn't even get a second opinion, and you tore the family apart by how meanly you came out as trans--"

I stood from the living room chair. “If this is your plan, there’s no reason for the conversation to continue. I’m going to Thailand, and I’m leaving right now.”

"Don't be like this."

"Don't call me." I walked out of the house and my dog, ever faithful, tried to follow me home. Looking back, I wish I'd have taken the pooch up on it. She was the only thing I ever wanted from that house, in retrospect.


## Addie, 2021

I kept it short and sweet when I texted my mom back:

"I've spent 14 years waiting for you to see that I'm good enough for you and every time you break my heart. I'm done. Lose my fucking number."

It was then I learned a new rule in my relationship with my mother: if you need a call from her, call her out. My phone started ringing off the hook.

I refused the first call. Immediate callback. Then two. Then three. Each call made me crumple even further into a ball, children inside howling with the terror that we had made mom angry, and that her anger meant we were the bad kid, and the only way to make things better was to do exactly what she wants— 

"I'm taking this," D said as they grabbed my phone and, bless their heart, tanked my sister's angry phone call for me. I heard them howling on the phone downstairs as I tried to calm down all the parts inside terrified that we did it wrong again, and everyone was going to blame us for it going bad. Just breathe, in, three, two, one, out…

---

# 8

# To Be Someone Else for One Day
Sometimes I get tired of doing the same thing
Sit still while the people around me are changing
One day I’m gonna find the feeling
What it’d be like
to be someone else for one day?

## Twi, 2021

Sometimes, when things are going terrible, you have to try a new life to get perspective on the one you live.

I have this, uh, thing I like to do. You know how some people like to travel? It’s sort of like that, but more. I’d leave the airport in Chicago alone and arrive in the host city an empty vessel who just moved to town. Instead of sightseeing, I wanted to make friends in the city, share their lives and, well, try to be someone else for one day.

In the aughts, my second life was as a girl in Chicago. In the teens, I was a polyamorous transbian immigrant to perennially weird Toronto. Here, at the end of my rope, I knew exactly what to reach for to get me back on the straight and narrow: a new adventure.

I never understood why we were like this. Maybe this was some weird wanderlust that I never quite got over while moving to Chicago. Maybe it was just my particular weird hobby. Maybe — (and by this he means “definitely” -Misty) — this was the way we made our divergent life paths work.

Being a system is about compromising. We all wanted different things out of life. Gender, career, relationships, hobbies, you can bet we disagree on what to focus our energies on. But out here, in a new city, I get to meet people for the first (and often last) time. With that many at-bats, we got to experiment with what kind of person we wanted to be as a whole system.

I can’t tell you how freeing that was: to know that we could be as inconsistent, weird, and out there as we wanted and _nobody would know about it at home_. It was raw energy in my veins.

Landing in Seattle, I felt like I grew ten years younger, I had so much energy.

This is all a long way of saying that, between the trouble at work and the mess with my dad, I decided to say fuck it, time for a trip to Seattle to see the Cats and meet a whole bunch of systems.

Over the course of the past year the Cats and I had struck up a bit of a relationship over text. At first it was her helping us out, but as we got to know each other as people (and as alters in systems!), we struck up a friendship that bubbled into a little bit of a romance.

That romance lead to meeting a whole new crew of trans, plural folks in Seattle, and the one thing I thought I could do to improve my mental health was fully immerse in the role. Be somewhere where, if you do fall apart, there will be no witnesses at home.

Yeah, my friends in Chicago were amazing and would never think to hurt me with anything I trusted them with, but. Well. Old habits die hard.

So I got the OK from my wife, bought a ticket out to Seattle, and planned to work remotely for the course of a full week with my trans, lesbian, furry, plural system friends. (Look, you picked up a book written by a blind, polyamorous, plural, autistic, furry lesbian. Let’s all be honest and say “we knew this was coming.”)

## Addie, 2011

…right. So in the teens, back when I was running around Toronto and working on how best to pronounce “sorry,” I was driving back home to Indiana and there was this line at the Michigan border. I’d just had the best weekend of my life hanging with my partners in the area, and to be honest, it’s still up there for “best weekends of my life.” (Hard to beat skinny dipping in Lake Ontario and playing an impromptu pop piano concert on the ferry ride home, but I digress.)

There we were, sitting in our little red Fiat and feeling homesick for Kensington Market and Queens Quay, and the thought occurred to us:

“We could just pull up tent stakes and move there.” Tally said. “We could marry Darq and just disappear and _be_ that weirdo who moved to Canada, if we wanted to be.”

“We have a job, though.”

“We could get a new job in Canada. Think about it! _Free healthcare._ Everyone you met made a point to mention it when you said you were from the states!”

I chuckled. “But there’s Chicago, too. Denis is there and he knows people.”

Denis was my college roommate, for reference. He also introduced me to D, so this is one of those butterfly effect moments that could have forever changed the trajectory of my life.

I knew I wasn’t going to convince Tally to go back to Indiana. We loved our friends there but after seeing what life in the big city could be like, we couldn’t waste another moment rotting in that town.

It was heads Toronto, tails Chicago: the only fair way to resolve the conflict in our system.

“American quarter or Canadian loonie?” I asked Tally. She was incredulous at first, I’ll give her that, but I pushed the question. “It’s the only fair way, Tally.”

“Loonie,” Tally said. “Can we put on ‘Heads Carolina, Tails California’ if we’re going to do this?”

“I’ll sing it for you,” I replied, and then hummed the tune while flipping the coin.

## Twi, 2021

All of my bravado, confidence, and ego went out the window the moment the Cats pulled up at the airport. It was one thing to talk to systems on the internet. Meeting a bunch of them in real  life was another thing entirely! I thought, briefly, about escape plans, but at this point I’d lost my driver’s license due to low vision, and getting the tickets would be expensive—

She rolled down the passenger window, leaned forward with her wispy, straight black hair, and yelled my name.

“Hey, Twi! Toss your bags in the back and let’s roll!”

…Yeah. she said _my_ name. I was right to be afraid of this one’s power.

“Excuse me?” I stammered. “How did you do that?”

The Cats flashed a big, warm smile. “You’re very easy to tell. And if you’re plural, it’s _really easy_ to tell your friends’ alters apart! Go ahead, try.”

I tossed my bags in the back to give myself some time to think. Sure enough, I could hear the sternness in her voice, the way her brow furrowed even when she smiled, the sharpness of her breath…

“Soli,” I said, and her smile only grew bigger. “Really? Okay. That’s, well, not what I was expecting. At all.”

“Why’s that?”

“I just wasn’t expecting it to be… uh, visible. Makes it hard to doubt the system’s existence.”

“Enjoy the validation,” Soli said, and then we sped off in her sporty little coupe toward Seattle, my new second home.

## Zoe, 2021

I was in a blue car zipping down a coastal highway. The sun told me it was early mid-day, but _I didn’t have my bags on me. Am I okay? Did someone take me? Oh god gosh I have to get—_

“Hey there!”

It was a lady’s voice, and one I recognized from Discord calls. The Cats! That means this is Seattle, and that means I’m safe. A knot in my stomach unwound.

“Oh!” I said, my face brightening a bit. “Sorry about that.”

“Don’t be sorry!” she said. “And don’t be embarrassed. This happens to people like us. It’s normal.”

“This is so weird.”

“Go On.”

“I remember waiting for the plane,” I said with a frown. “And now I’m just here.”

“Yep! That’s completely normal for you.” She reached for the console. “Let me play you something. There’s a DJ I like who has DID. Do you want to hear what one of their alters put together?”

She played a mashup album that was somewhere between too fast and too overwhelming, but as we adjusted to the overload the entire system sprang to attention to rock out to something I could only explain in the moment as “music for systems, by a system.” The music was nostalgic but new, as much of it’s sound was sampled from 90s video games and top 10 hits. I had a blast naming all the references as we zoomed around the sound.

Around us, the skyline of Seattle peeked out from behind a nearby hill, its skyscrapers long fingers on a hand that waved a welcoming “hello” to me as we sped toward our new second home.

## Sam, 2021

They - and by they, I mean Soli, and all the other cats, and her girlfriend Aimee - lived in a garden-level, one bedroom unit on Capitol Hill. For added “hip young people in the big city sitcom” cred, their apartment included a sliding window that functioned as a second entrance.

It reminded me of my own apartment in the 20s in all the best ways. A rotating cast of characters came through that sliding door; most of whom I already knew through long chats over Discord where we discussed everything from system life to transit proposals for far-off cities.

The most striking thing was all of them were just _there_, openly mentally ill and supporting one another. They talked about their challenges, and knew how to both accept and receive support when their turn came up.

And IDK, maybe that was what I needed. To be around others like me, to feel like I wasn’t a burden on my friends who didn’t have a crippling mental disability like DID. I needed a place where I could just be another messed-up individual among a community of likewise-messed up folk, and know that nothing bad was going to happen.

“We regularly break fire code here,” Soli joked. “Between my twenty, Aimee’s ten, your…” She paused. “However many you actually have in there.”

That caught me off guard and she read my facial experssion.

She chided me with a gentle wave of her hand. “I’ve had conversations with your alters, you dork. There’s stuff you don’t know yet, but I bet you will soon. You know why?”

“Why?”

“Because you’re going to let your guard down this vacation.”

Something about her tone of voice made me want to simultaneously obey her and refuse her. On one hand, how dare she just out and _say_ something like that, without considering all the possible angles?

And on the other, how did she say it in a way that made me feel safe and loved?

How did she turn what should have been an insult into an endearing way to say “I support you”?

Soli’s eyes crossed for a moment and her hands went limp. 
A switch. The voice that came out was still the one I remembered, but different. Gone was the confidence and husky alto. Instead, I was looking at a hyper-feminine trans woman living her absolute best life in gender euphoria.

Of course Lexi would come out to break the tension.

“Oh my gosh, you’re here!” She hugged me just as if we hadn’t ridden in the car together. And I guess, in a very real way, we didn’t; that ride belonged to Soli, Twi, and Zoe. Taking that dream logic into account made everything click into place.

The joy present in that room quickly pushed the thought away, though, as both myself and the Cats started rapid-switching to introduce themselves in person. By the time everyone had come up to say hello, we crashed onto the couch, stricken with the vertigo of being a dozen different people within the span of a minute.

There was a safe, cozy energy in the apartment as her cuddle-friendly friends slowly introduced themselves and joined us on the couch

An entire room of trans feminine women, denied their childhoods and raised on hard masculinity, were collectively regaining their ability to give and receive physical affection. There was something very… well, community-focused, obviously, about the whole moment. But it was intensely healing, too.

“Wanna teach me how to play pinball?”

“Thought you’d never ask,” I said, and the cuddle pile dissolved into a bunch of free trans radicals, all excited energy and no place to put it other than to run, run, hard and fast into something so joyful that it could cause our hearts to explode with joy.

## Addie, 2021

“You know we broke the fire code by coming here.”

It was Nebulosa making the joke this time. She’s another trans, lesbian system living on Capitol Hill. Here at Shorty’s, a divey joint near Pike Place Market, she wore her dark brown hair in a low ponytail to better emphasize her fashion choice of the day: a low-key starship fighter pilot uniform. (Which she pulled off *fabulously* by the way.)

She pointed to a sign about total occupancy that I pretended to be able to see. “Just there’s, like, so many alters among all these systems that we’re definitely over a 192 occupants.”

Chuckling at bad system jokes felt good. I hadn’t realized how much shame I felt about my own system experiences when I was back home. Maybe it’s because my friends remembered a different, more stable version of me, and I didn’t want them to feel burdened by this.

“So they do great hot dogs here,” Nebulousa continued. “I think they even do a Chicago style if you want a taste of home.

I looked to the menu of this Seattle dive, hoping I could read the text. No dice. However, something truly unexpected hung on that wall: a mustard-yellow Vienna Beef sign. My Chicago transplant-ass nearly fell out of the chair.

“You’re kidding.”

Lexi touched my shoulder with concern. “What’s wrong?”

Nebulosa looked nervous. “Something I said?”

“One second,” I said, and flagged the bartender down. “Hi. Do you do Chicago Handshakes?”

The bartender’s smile curled into a wicked grin.

“Twelve of those, please,” I said, smiling. “I got a lot of folks to induct to the club.”

“What’s that?” Nebulosa asked.

“It’s a Chicago thing,” I said, and left it at that. “So, you’re a system too?”

“Most of us here are.” We made our way over to a table to wait for the shots. Inside the plexiglass recess of the table was a dilapidated pinball playfield, and I idly traced my fingers along its surface, as if playing pinball in my head.

_They’re all just like me._

“Wild.” I took in the folks in the crowd surrounding me with a sense of awe. “I’m one of, like, two plurals I know in Chicago, and one of those I’m dating.”

Lexi popped her head over my shoulder. “We tend to find each other, you know. Smart kids. Outcasts. Weird kids. We build our own tribe. And it helps to be around other systems, but I can tell by the way you’re relaxing you already knew that.

“Did you want something to drink?”

“I bought a round for everyone,” I said, smiling. “As a thank-you.”

“You didn’t.”

Nebulosa looked at me, her new friend, with a mixture of scorn, curiosity, and concern.

I winked. “They had it. I’m required by Chicago law to do this. Act natural.”

Lexi shook her head. Nebulosa laughed. “Count me in,” she said, and bellied up to the bar to wait for her round.

It was at that point another trans woman made a point to say hello. She was close to my age, polite, and internet-poisoned in all the right ways for a degenerate like myself to love. She introduced herself as the Helixes, and we went through the whole handshake-and-share-system-names back and forth that is apparently standard operating procedure within plural communities.

I want to say that I was getting used to it, but the Helixes had me read for _filth._ She made a point of it, too: with one of her warm, calm hands she took my shoulder and gave it a squeeze.  I didn’t fight the touch and was rewarded with a friendly, gentle squeeze. “Gosh!” she said. “You’re wound up so so tight!

“You’re safe here, you know? Like, nobody is going to hurt you in Seattle. Nobody even *knows you*.”

She left to join the others and I’m not going to lie, it took some time to absorb and process that.

The drinks came out. For those of you who haven’t gone on the Chicago Handshake journey I won’t spoil it here; suffice it to say a great experience was had by all and everyone immediately ordered a second round.

I hissed as the liquor hit my stomach. Something about the burn of alcohol and the smell of cheap Old Style stirred something ancient within me.

There was wood paneling on the walls. Shag carpet dirty with sweat and feet. We were choking and could swear we heard laughter. Our body began to shake and the bar fell away. I wandered in a daze as the world around me gave way to overwhelming static and an animalistic urge to _run_

_It’s happening again. It’s happening and I have to run I have to lose them or else it’s gonna happen again and I don’t want it I don’t want it I don’t want it_

## Twi, 2021

“Come on. Keep breathing. You’re safe.”

I came to standing at street corner in the chilly late summer Seattle air. To my side was the Cats - Lexi, I thought - who had apparently followed me out. She dug around her purse with a quiet intensity. Downtown Seattle was, well, downtown on a weekday evening: empty, save for some bikers and cabbies returning from uptown fares.

“You back with me yet?”

The sound made me jump. “Lexi?”

“No, you dork. Your bud Soli.” A second look at the Cats’ face confirmed it; for all intents and purposes Lexi was really and truly Not there. If I tried to talk to Lexi about this conversation she would literally be at a loss to summarize what happened.

This fucking disorder, man.

“You know it’s me, then.”

“Like I said, you’re very easy to tell.” She plucked something from her purse and gave me a huge hug. “You doing okay?”

“I’m fine.”

“You just went wide-eyed and ran out of a bar. Here, have some system first aid.”

She revealed, pinched between her fingers, a fresh cone joint and a lighter.

“This is ‘Cheetah Piss Cookies.’ You’re gonna think I’m crazy, but I promise you it will help. You’ll thank me later.”

A joint. I was coming back from a flashback and her fix was sparking up a joint. I’m not going to lie, my first instinct was to say “how West-coast of you,” but I swallowed the joke for fear she’d go away.

The last thing I wanted was to be alone with my thoughts on that street corner.

I shrugged and opened my hand. “Fuck it. Let’s smoke this joint.”

She pulled the joint away as I grabbed for it. “You’re not allowed to stop until I tell you to.”

“The hell?”

“Trust me.” She lit the joint and passed it to me. “It’s gonna seem like a lot, I know.”

I could feel my heart pounding hard against my ribcage, as if it were trying to break free. “If you told me it’d help my nerves I’d stand on my head and do a gravity bong like back in college.”

“Good boy,” she said, winking. Fuck, she even knew what gender the alters were and changed her pronouns to match! “Now. Three good puffs.”

"That's a lot of puffs. You're sure?" I took a drag on the joint and willed it to work, to make the bad trailer park in my mind go away. Honestly, with the way I felt inside I'd have done anything Soli said to make the image disappear.

"One more," Soli said. "I promise. It seems like a lot but you need it right now."

I crushed half a joint on my own based on the advice of a twentysomething system I sort of had the hots for. I was 38 years old at the time.

This fucking disorder.

It took a few minutes before the weed worked its magic. The dirty, nasty room with wood paneling was still there, still nagging at the edge of my thoughts. That would never change. But at least now it was less overwhelming and nightmareish. I could put it away for now and it wouldn’t consume me.

"You're good now," Soli said. "Your shoulders just dropped a full inch from relaxing so hard."

I took a deep breath and found the energy to smile. "Wait, the world feels different now. Like, it's closer. Smaller. And if I touch a wall I don't have that weird feeling like I'm going to pass through it without making contact."

"That's called being grounded," Soli said with a smile. "You may not like to hear it, but you have severe trauma that affects your ability to function like a neurotypical human being. This stuff will help you when the symptoms get bad."

"Nothing we've been through was that bad. I mean, there are some unkind things, yes, and there's that big empty hole in my memory that I can't push through, but I'm okay. I'm not disabled or anything, and I certainly don't have trauma bad enough to warrant that kind of breakdown. It's obviously my fault."

Soli took my hand with a laugh and led me back toward the bar. "It isn't. And you have severe trauma; The others have told me."

"...oh, right."  

"Yeah, hard to remember things you're not the only one with control, ainnit?"  

I nodded. "It's so weird."

"The alters?" Soli shook her head. "No, that's normal for you. It's just an adjustment, is all."

"No, the weed I smoked. I don't feel high."

"Yeah," Soli said. "That's a good sign you needed it. Most people, they smoke weed and go from 'normal' to 'high.' People like us? Who use this to manage PTSD? We go from 'extremely triggered' to 'normal.'

"You get used to it, I promise. But for now, teach me some pinball, eh?"

## Addie, 2021

Shorty's was a crowded place at the best of times, but when it crowded with my kind of people, I felt at home.

The bar was built by a Chicago transplant who wanted to bring the Chicago dive to the west coast. The entire lot was cut in half by a dividing wall to suggest the thin, long lots Chicago is famous for. On one side was a bar, hot dog stand, and some fancy tables. On the other: pristine pinball machines, crowded into a tiny space, many of which I had never played before.

I was in my happy place to say the least, and as the hours passed turned back to my pre-system self, dancing between people and generally holding court like a 20's kid who finally found their crowd. Each of my new friends played a different machine and I'd observe them from the side, bringing up little tips and tricks as they drained balls. And to my delight, everyone seemed to have a good time. Some even got involved in their local league, which makes me ever so happy.

Happy. Not just a fleeting feeling anymore, happiness came with a sense of safety and belonging. Look, I get why the psych community doesn't want systems to talk to each other. We trigger each other super easily, and while we can take care of each other these episodes can have a chain-reaction effect, taking out every system in the room.

But here? Surrounded by systems who also survived the kinds of hell we survived? Screw the doctors. It was nice to be just another face in the crowd than being the craziest person in the group.

In a weird way, it was just nice to belong.

I felt a hand on my shoulder, and there was Lexi. (It didn't take me long to get used to reading the Cats the way they read me, turns out.) She wore concerned eyes and a huge grin.  

"Doing okay? Soli told me what happened."

"...right, Twi and the kiddo. You know, the strangest thing just happened. I had almost forgotten it."

"That gets better with time," she said, and then pointed to my squinting eyes. "Oh, Soli gave you the Cheetah Piss Cookies! Knew that weed would work for you. Honestly, any cookie strain is great for PTSD--"

"Hold on."

"And I'm having fun with pinball, too! I knew it was gonna be fun but it's so, so cool when you know what's going on?"

"No, it's weird. I feel fine. Happy, even! But I also know that I just ran out of this place like a wide-eyed, lost little child."

I turned to look at her with wide, confused eyes. "How do you deal with this day after day?"

Her eyes fluttered for a second and there was Soli again, eyes furrowed and stern. "First? The word you're looking for isn't 'like.' You _were_ a scared little child running out of a noisy, strange bar, in a very real and very inescapable way. The sooner you accept that, the easier this journey will be."

"And second?"

"Tell that dork Twi to stop ruminating on it. You're a system, and you're beautiful just the way y'all are.”

We played a few more rounds of pinball as the night grew long, but my head was somewhere else entirely. Not quite "anxious to make new friends," and not quite "surely these folks are going to hurt me eventually." It was something new entirely:

_Safety._  

The bar filled with the late night crowd and the crew decided to head home for the night. We got a bag of Dick’s and the whole dirty dozen of trans, poly, plural folk piled into the Cats' apartment. We stayed there and talked until dawn peeked over the country and painted Puget Sound with its early morning light.

## Sam, 2021

In the haze of dawn’s early light the crew headed to their homes. Meanwhile, I made myself busy getting comfortable on the couch for a good afternoon’s sleep.

“What are you doing?” Lexi asked.

“Sleeping, I thought.”

Lexi’s girlfriend Aimee laughed. “You’re welcome to join us if you want.”

So my girlfriends and I slept together that night. It’s no metaphor; we just cuddled and slept. And being there with them, knowing that my system was safe no matter what, I slept better than I had in a long while.

## Twi, 2021

Being away from home meant I could mess with my gender, too. Most days I in Chicago I didn’t get to dress up the way that I wanted to. Not as a person, but as an alter; a dapper dude and wannabe bartender. But here? Freed from any long-term consequences for my experimentation? You better believe I was going to go for guy mode.

That’s how I ended up in a soup dumpling joint with my new girlfriend Aimee, wearing a button-up, a binder, and the tiniest bit of beard shadow. Yes, the others had just spent the past 15 years trying to _not_ look like this. I wish I had an explanation for how that worked, but I’m just a guy. Sorry if that’s weird.

“How are you liking Seattle so far?”

Aimee posed the question. She had this cute smile when she asks it because she already knew the answer; I love it here. The people are weird, there’s lots of pinball, and everyone I’ve met has been an absolute treat.

“You don’t have to answer,” Aimee said with a wink.

“Oh, it’s nice! It’s got me thinking about moving.”

Look, we didn’t all want to end up in Chicago. We’re still over-the-moon happy here, but there’s a lot of fun in thinking about the what-may-have-beens. What would the system look like if Tally won the coin flip and we’d spent the past 15 years absorbing as much Canadiana as humanly possible? What if — oh gods — what if I never left Indiana?

But imagining life as a Seattlite if I were the only one driving the body? Sure, the resulting mess would have been life-ruining in the real world, but it was fun to run with my little version of the dream for a little while.

“Oh, there’s an apartment upstairs that’s for rent. You and D could move in.”

I remembered, in that moment, reading case studies about systems who ended up in a fugue state and, in that state, made some massive decisions. (I mean, “one way international flight” levels of big decisions.) I’ve run away from friends before without meaning to, only to come back wearing clothes I didn’t own before. Or there have been projects I’ve been told I did that I can’t remember doing.

Moments like this, where my heart literally filled to bursting with the desire to start a new life, made those wild, unbelievable stories of fugue state travel a little more believable. The last thing I needed to do was give into the impulse, so I laughed and waved the table off.

“I love the city and I want to visit, but I have a home and a wife in Chicago. And an entire found family!”

Amy’s friends’ eyes widened. “Oh, we were just messing around.”

“I know, I know.” I laughed and stuffed a shrimp roll in my face to buy some time. “Give us a second.”

Us. Yeah, we used plural language around strangers sometimes. Partially because we forget to mask and pretend to be a single person around others. Partially because here I knew there was no harm in messing up a new relationship because of my plurality.

I finished up the roll and started again. “So, one of the things about this condition is that I can’t remember what I did from day to day. In fact, I don’t even know what or who I want to be from moment to moment, because we all want different things. I love thinking about it, but I gotta keep the future bias in check, you know?

“I’ve made a great life for myself, and I want to make Seattle my, uh,” I counted on my fingers for effect, “third home, after Chicago and Toronto.”

Aimee lifted her martini and clinked it against my glass. “Couldn’t have said it better myself,” she said with a chuckle, and downed a good swig of the martini. 

She hissed as the alcohol burned its way down and we all shared a good laugh about the fact that I very nearly agreed to move here without even contacting my wife.

(Sorry, honey, things get weird in the moment!)

## Zoe, 2021

I was full of really good Chinese food and hanging out with my friend Rosie that night. She was part of Aimee but now that we’re not at the fancy Chinese place we get to be kids with each other and that was super nice.

We laid on the couch and held hands and whispered secrets to each other. “We can pretend it’s a sleepover,” Rosie said, and I was super excited to get out sleeping bags and popcorn but we didn’t have any.

Rosie told me some really sad things that happened to her, and I told her some sad things that happened to me. It felt good to share. I like sharing. It makes the bad stuff hurt your heart less.

(Around us, a small segment of the Seattle crew watched as the two of us acted like literal four year olds on the couch. “That’s Rosie and Zoe,” Lexi said to the others. “They’re both very little and want to be friends. I’m sure the adults will be back in a second.” I could have come up to explain that yes, I’ll be back in a second, but Zoe was having waaay too much fun to interrupt. -Addie)
