“I’m glad I got a friend my age,” Rosie said. Our eyes met and there, caught in the twinkling of the eyes of a thirtysomething body being driven by a system kid, was glassy-eyed longing. We were in Seattle, sure, and we were adults. But in that moment it was just me and Rosie, hurt kids hoping to make friends!

So we linked pinkies and swore to be best friends forever.

“That’s good,” I said with a smile. “You’ll stay a while now. I can’t, uh, keep friends.”

“Aww.” She grabbed my hands and squeezed. “Well, you’re going to be nice and safe here I promise! There’s adults watching us so we can just be ourselves without worrying about it.”

“Promise?”

“Promise.” She squeezed my hands harder. “You’re safe here, Zoe. I double promise.”

I flashed a big smile. “Okay. Can we make music then?”

## Addie, 2021

I came to with the body already in motion, so I tried my best to be still and watch from a dissociated distance.

The system kids were kinds of like scared little bunnies in that way - if you showed the tiniest bit of interest in them, they were gone. But here, surrounded by new friends in a place that my old family and abusers couldn't get to? I could relax and let the kids do their thing.

I was on the Cats' large couch, holding hands with Aimee. There was this innocence in the way we made that contact, as if it were the first time we let someone in and it still felt so new and *exciting*  to everyone inside!

Sure enough, there she was making beats on Twi's little groovebox. He got it for our birthday a year or so back, "just to see if I like it," and now it goes everywhere we go. The thing with Zoe, though, was how effortless and *good*  she was at making music. Without so much as a hitch in her step her fingers slid across the lighted pads, and out came what she described as "house music for a trashy sushi bar."

It also made me feel terribly silly, realizing that an alter in my system knows how to make music as easily as one breathes and, try as I may, I wasn't allowed to access that power. So many little talents hiding in here, all held back by barriers meant to keep the bad stuff from destroying us.

“Just like how our planning ability goes to shit when you aren't in front,” Misty added.

No wonder we don't trust ourselves anymore. Who would, when what you're able to do depends on the day and the alter fronting?

"I think she's just excited to meet other littles," Lexi said. She was speaking with another plural we met in Seattle. The Helixes stopped by to say hello; we had plans to go out for a hike in a large forest preserve the next day. I guess she came over to hang out, but as soon as she saw the two littles on the couch she kept busy with Lexi.

We must have been a sight to see, with Rosie and Zoe playing like toddlers while the adults looked on, chuckling.

"Hi Helix!" Zoe said. She waved this big, silly, childlike wave.

"Hi Zoe-bo-bantha!" Alex replied. "Having fun?"

"Wait." The world snapped into focus and I shot up ramrod-straight. "Oh god, I'm so sorry. I must look like an idiot over here, pretending to be a kid--"

Lexi's eyes turned stern. "Addie! Hey! Zoe was taking her turn fronting."

"It's really rude to keep your littles down like that," Helix added. "They are just as real as the adult alters are, and they deserve to make safe decisions too."

"Yeah, that." Her voice turned back to sing-song: "Go back to making friends with Rosie, Zoe! The adults are just chatting. You're safe!"

That's all the more encouragement Zoe needed to pop back out. There was no discussion, no negotiation: one second I was in control and ashamed, and the next I was just, well, Zoe, 9 years old and feeling really safe. Something about being around other systems plus everyone being at the internet-strangers stage of making friends made me relax enough for her to take back control.

Potts put a hand on my shoulder. “Be proud of her. When is she going to have the chance to do this again, Addie? Play with another kid just like her? Relax. This is normal for people like us.”

Zoe's smile carved deep dimples onto my cheeks. The beat mutated under her fingers: first into a slow shuffle, then disco, until she was making etherial ambient patterns to twiddle parameter dials to.

Across the couch, Rosie watched the groovebox's lights dance to and fro. "That's neat. Did you know I played guitar?"

"No! We should jam out sometime!"

We listened in silence for a few moments more.

"You're gonna be a real good friend," Rosie said. It wasn't pointed at the system as a whole; in fact, I could already feel the memory slipping away in the moment.
  
Zoe’s face turned grim. "I'm terrified of when the rug gets pulled."

"What rug?"

"When you find out who I really am, of course." Zoe chuckled. "Once people do that they stop coming around."

Rosie's face fell. "There is no rug pull, friends."

"I just can't keep friends. I don't know why."

Rosie gave my hands a squeeze. "We're not going anywhere."

Zoe looked over to the adults in the room to make sure I saw them nodding in agreement.

## Addie, 2021

"Magic mushrooms just, like, *grow*  in Seattle. If you know what you're looking for you can just pick 'em."

Alex pointed out good patches for the shrooms as we walked through the cool air and beautiful vistas of the Seattle Arboretum. Wrens sang their wandering song overhead, and my footsteps released petrichor as we walked. Of _course_ magic mushrooms just grew here; what else could one expect from a place as weird as Seattle?

She walked the paths with a familiarity that we envied, honestly. To know a space so completely that every sight, every corner, every moment could be visualized as if going off some internal map. Me, I had my cane and was doing my best to tap along. It was still early, and I tripped on a few roots here and there, but I’d like to think I did a decent job navigating.

The first thing you notice about Helix is just how, well, *gender*  they all are. In the span of an hour you could see everything from hyperfemme diva to withdrawn shy boy, and every combination in between. We had talked about our trauma in broad strokes and decided it was better for both of us if we just left it at "we are way too similar to not have the same trauma.”

We stopped at the top of a hill that overlooked the sound. And me, autistic as usual, jumped right into it. “So. How's it feel to be in the 'mental health breakdown in your 30s' club?"

The Helixes laughed at that. “Right into it, then.”

“I’m just curious,” I said. “How did it go down for you?”

We walked down into a lightly forested ravine. Above, a plane approached SeaTac, its engine noise scattering the wrens for a good long spell.

“I found my system while I was working as a coder. And I was good at my job, too - I was the senior you called when you needed someone to fix a sticky technical solution. But finding out about the others made me realize why conventional therapy wasn’t working, so it was good at first!”

I nodded along. We walked around a bend that overlooked a deep valley and we paused there, looking out onto the grey horizon.

“It started with a lack of focus, I guess. Used to churn through code like it was no big thing, but as Covid went on I just… I just couldn’t anymore. Then there were the flashbacks, and the memory loss, and, well…”

They started to walk again.

“I asked for medical leave, they gave it to me. Then I kept finding more alters, and more alters, and fragments of things that used to be alters. Some days I don’t know which way is up. Some days I’m a kid trapped in a human body; others, I’m barely getting by without having more brain issues.”

I took a deep breath and hissed. Talking about it from the inside was one thing. Hearing it out of someone else’s mouth, and realizing that it applied to you? That’s a different thing entirely.

“I know your pain,” I said at last, and we kept walking.

“How about you?”

“Oh, I’m still employed, so that’s good. I’m really lucky to be remote right now so that I can work on getting the system under control and settled.”

“I take it it’s not going well?”

“To say the least.”

We walked in silence for a few moments more before I realized they were doing the same “stay quiet until you open up” trick I did earlier and honestly, *how dare they.* I managed a laugh in the quiet: “Guess I’m not getting out of talking about my problems, am I?”

“Nope!”

“Gah, okay. Fine. So yes, I have a job right now. It’s pretty nice, all told, and pays well. But I know I’m not as good at the job as I used to be. It’s the little things I keep missing. The meetings I forget. The balls I drop, you know? Stuff that should be easy but is insanely hard when you have coworkers in your head.”

Ahead, I could hear a runner approaching on the right. I stopped talking until she passed.

“So that's the hard part, I guess. I *know* I'm worse at my job than I was before. It's like... it's like I can remember *who*  I used to be, and what I used to be capable of, but for the life of me I can't recognize that person in myself right now."

"Like you were replaced?"

I considered that for a moment. "No. Like I'm broken. Like the person I was before can never exist again, because to do so would kill me.”

Helix nodded. “Same. It’s an adjustment, after spending your life being the high-flyer, isn’t it?”

“Yeah. I just know that the person that comes out of this,” I tapped my cane hard on the concrete for emphasis, “The, well, *we* that comes out of this will be different. Probably less flashy. Definitely less work-focused. More chill, for sure.”

“I don’t know who you are going to be, but I know you’re amazing right now. want a hug?”

I took it. Helixes give the best hugs.

“It’s funny,” I said, awkwardly trying to change the subject, “The more folks I meet here, the more I want to move here!”

“You can’t, sweetie.” She pointed to my cane. “There’s only a few walkable neighborhoods and transit isn’t advanced enough to cover for that. You’d be stuck at home all day.”

“Oh.”

“Sorry,” Helix said. “I didn’t mean for it to come off that way.”

I laughed it off as best I could. “You’re fine! Not like I’m going to *do* any of this; I have a home, a mortgage, and an amazing wife. I’m happy as a clam. But sometimes I like to, uh, look at how my life tree got pruned, you know?”

They took a seat on a nearby bench. “Tell me more.”

“I imagine growth in life kind of like pruning a tree. If you want branches in one place, you gotta prune back other spaces to encourage the growth. And like, as a kid you can grow in a million different ways, but every time you do you prune a little bit away from what could have been. Maybe those extra video games as kids means we won’t be piano prodigies. Get a BA, and you cut off an entire world of people you wouldn’t have otherwise met. You cut and cut and the tree grows the way you direct it, however twisted and gnarled it may become.

“Every time I come to, I realize someone else in my head has trimmed away branches.” I held up my cane. “Sometimes, I don’t get to make the call on which branch to cut, sure. But the mangled tree that came of me? with all its gnarls and whorls and stray branches? It’s a mess to look at.

“In Seattle, each of us inside gets to experiment with what could have been. Twi can be a dude; I can learn about mushrooms on a hike. Zoe gets to be, well, _Zoe_. Of course we want to stay here forever!”

“She’s a cutie,” Helix said with a shrug. “Can’t argue with that.”

“I just… yeah. I’m happy with how my tree ended up overall. Do I wish the others made different decisions? Yes. (I’d love to find out who tossed out our piano in 2013, for example.) But when I’m here, we can all be different people with different ways of *being alive* and honestly, it feels great. Would the illusion break if I had to do shit like ‘find a job’ and ‘figure out the whole ‘ope, now I gotta convince my wife to go to Seattle’ conversations? You bet! But this isn’t about being feasible. This is about trying on a new identity.

“That’s all, I guess. I’m just playing pretend for a while before I have to back to the real world.”

Helix put a hand on our shoulder and gave a squeeze. “Okay. Two things. One, *please* go see a massage therapist!” She squeezed again, hard, until my neck relaxed. “Seriously, you’re the tightest person I’ve ever met.”

“I bear my heart to you and that’s the first thing I get?” I chuckled. It had only been a few days knowing Helix but I feel like I have a long-lost sister in her. “You weirdo. ‘Sorry about the heartbreak and ennui and stuff but *girl get a massage*! You are catastrophically gay, girl.”

“Guilty!” she rolled her eyes. “Anyway. Right. This ‘new identity’ you’re experimenting with? It’s not a new thing. _This is what DID is_, friend. Never knowing who you are, or who you want to be, until you arrive on the scene and someone inside takes over. You have *always been this way*, Addie, and the sooner you accept that the sooner things will get better.”

I was speechless.

She patted my shoulder. “Now come on; let’s get you home. You wanted to hit the grocery store, right? I can drive you. Come on, slowpoke! Sun’s going down soon!”

## Potts, 2021

The Cats’ apartment filled with the smell of searing meat and roasting potatoes. I had headphones on so that I could listen to showtunes at full volume and mouth the words.

“What’s that smell?” Lexi asked, but I couldn’t hear. Aimee tapped my shoulder to get my attention.

I took off my headphones. “Oh! Sorry. Didn’t want to bother folks, but uh, what’s the point of cooking if you can’t sing and dance, I say.”

“You could put that on for everyone.” Lexi said.

“Yeah. But it’s Broadway today, and I know that’s not everyone’s cup of tea.”

Aimee agreed. “If you like it, we want to listen to it!”

“Okay, but I warn you, I’ve been singing for 30 years and I’m a total nerd about this stuff.” I pulled out my phone, worried over my music library, and threw “Being Alive” to their speakers.

“That didn’t answer my question, though. What’s all this?”

“Sunday dinner– wait, this part is so so good!” I pointed to the speaker and started singing along. “Sorry, this is a fun musical.”

Lexi laughed. “You’re fine!”

“Right. So, y’all made me feel so welcome and so seen as, well, a system. I wanted to do something to thank you, and the first thing that came to mind was sharing a family dinner. So:” I pointed to each pot, “pork tenderloin, roast potatoes, gravy, and a warm brussels sprouts salad.”

Aimee whistled. “Wow, what a spread!”

“Thanks!” I beamed. “Cooking is sort of my thing in the system. I picked it up as a hobby shortly after our family disowned us in ‘08. Mom never taught me to cook so I learned from who I could.” I spun back toward the tenderloin and gave it a flip. The sharp scent of roasted herbs and nutty butter perfumed the air.

“This is basically my childhood,” I said. (Well, I didn’t say it. A little teenage girl inside took my voice from me, but you know how that goes, &c &c.) “At my mom’s house we’d play music really loud and sing along. Mom made sure we got the notes right. And she cooked really nice Sunday roasts that we’d sit around and just, like, talk  about our day.”

“That sounds beautiful.”

“It was nice while it lasted.”

My eyes lowered as I thought about all the family dinners I’d missed over the years because my family simply could not  handle the fact I was trans, among other things.

Lexi pointed to the stereo. “It wasn’t all bad, was it? Now you’re showing us the things you love and that’s cool!”

“That’s what makes everything harder,” I responded. “I love  this vibe. Cooking, singing, chatting with new family? Can’t get any better. But I can’t help remembering what it used to be like, you know, when things were good.”

“And they weren’t always good.”

“Yeah. Help me with this, would you?” I held out a plastic bag to Lexi. “Just hold this open. I’m gonna sous vide the meat.”

“Sounds fancy.”

“My twist on family dinner,” I said with a smile.

The crew from Shorty’s trickled in as I cooked, each making their entrance via the half-height sliding door that functioned as a porch. Some gave me a huge hug, knowing that they wouldn’t see me after today. Others talked about the spread, the music, and video games. Always videogames, with some of these folks, and it suited them just fine!

We crowded around every table the apartment had to offer. Folks crowded around the dining room table, the sectional couch and coffee table, and even a few folks sat on the ground to share the meal. We sipped at wine from mason jars and mix-and-match glasses from the cupboard and told stories from our pasts.

Inside, we felt bubbly. Happy, even! Nobody from my past knew where I was, nobody could contact me unless I let them, and if I decided this was all to embarrassing to think about I could just delete the memory entirely. It felt, well, _freeing_ to have a chance to define myself again, free from any past in Chicago and everywhere else.

That anonymity was at the heart of everything, I think. Even this family dinner was, in a way, an anonymous way to gain family. Out here, nobody can see my mistakes. Out here, people could be held at arm’s length whenever I needed to. Out here, the people I met literally could never  hurt me; at the first sign of an issue I could just walk away, no harm done, back to my wife and family in Chicago.

Out here, surrounded by anonymity, I wasn’t afraid to open up.

When I was a little kid I had this love of costumes. I’d dress up in whatever fun costumes were around and play pretend for a while. Somewhere around elementary school, though, I fell in love with the idea of “invisible” costumes. Something to completely erase my personhood so that I was an empty shell of a person, ready to take on anything without my past failures being dredged up.

I mean, I grew up femme-presenting male in a small town in Indiana. The teasing was pretty overwhelming, and I learned quickly to never show my true self to the world. A town like that gives abusers eyes and ears everywhere, and the only safe solution to the problem was to invent a “false self” that people could fall in love with.

Somewhere along the way, I lost track of who I wanted to be, replaced instead with who I was *supposed* to be. Out here, freed from any expectations, I could reclaim that genuine self. And, if I wanted to, I could bring it home.

*I could let my found family in, if I wanted.*

We finished dinner in quiet contemplation. It was an old skill we learned growing up; be an active listener so you can’t get yelled at for spacing out, but every time you open your mouth you just so happen to deflect to someone else. I’m not proud of it, but we needed the space.

A fight was brewing inside.

“We could come here more often,” Zoe offered. Around us, the crew finished their dinner and busied themselves with doing up the dishes.

“Everyone’s really nice here,” Misty said.

“So is our family back home,” Addie offered. “We’re just so busy trying to show them we’re getting better than we didn’t even offer to let them in.”

“But here, we’re one of many, uh, manys. They get us.”

“Our family could get us,” I said, “if we let them in.”

And then we just fell out.

## Sam, 2021

One of the more embarrassing side effects of DID is the occasional child throwing a temper tantrum. When it happens I can only look on in horror, shame, and a little delight as a literal child cuddled tight to Alex and started to bawl.

Nobody reacted with shock. Indeed, everyone went about their business as if nothing was going on. Kids just have tantrums sometimes, and that’s part and parcel with being a system.

A child inside of me welled up to the surface. “I’m gonna miss Rosie!” they screamed, and then began to wail.

Helix took my hand and led me to a room for some privacy. “Hey sweetie! You’re safe! What’s up?”

“I got to be little with her and she’s so nice! And I’m gonna miss you too! And I’m gonna miss Shorty’s, and Lexi, and Aimee, and Nebulosa, and–“

“I know, I know kiddo. I bet it’s really scary, meeting new friends and not knowing when you’ll be back.”

I nodded. “A lot of my friends stopped coming around when I was growing up. It’s scary.”

“Can I promise you something?”

“Uh-huh?”

“I promise, the adults in your system are gonna bring you back! And I bet you there’s a whole mess of friends and family back home that would be happy seeing you being just the way you are!”

“Really?”

“Promise,” Helix said, and I made her pinky-swear on it.

## Addie, 2021

I came to back in the living room, my arms wrapped around Helix like one might handle a bit of driftwood after a shipwreck. My eyes were moist - again - and the room had gone quiet.

“Back to reality?” Helix asked.

“I think so.” I said. My brain felt like it was full of cotton. The world distorted in strange, dark tones, as if my brain were forcing itself to believe this was just an intense dream. After all, we don’t have little kids living inside our head who hold onto horrible, horrible things! That just can’t be possible.

But there I was, tears still wet on Helix’s shirt, and the evidence was unavoidable. “I’m sorry,” I said to everyone, but no one batted an eye.

“That’s just Tuesday,” Lexi said. “Last week I had a breakdown at an arcade with some friends. Like, full on breakdown. Screaming, crying, lost in trauma time.”

“Oh.”

“We’re used to this kind of stuff.” She waved to us from the door. “You don’t look okay. Can I get you off of the couch and onto the patio? I think you need some air and some grounding exercises to come back to reality.”

She was right; the world around me had the veneer of static. I was tired, despite being wide awake; a sure sign the switches weren’t going to end anytime soon. We even started to wonder if we were awake at all; that perhaps this whole trip happened in a single, disjointed dream.

So I let her take me by the hand and walk me into the chill Seattle nighttime air. The world snapped back into focus and I was back, visibly an adult and fully in control of my faculties. “Never gets old,” I said as I stretched and reminded myself that the body existed in real, three-dimensional space in 2022, not some dreamy dissociated soup of unreal past.

She lit up a joint. “You’re mad triggered,” she said, offering it to me.

Given the choice between ping-ponging between littles and adults all night and being stoned, I took the latter. A long drag later I could feel my muscles relax. The ground stopped feeling like it would give way at any moment and for the moment, I had control.

Inside, the kids needled at my consciousness. It felt like dozens - no, hundreds! - of little children were inside me, locked away in a vault, and the kids had finally broken containment. My skin quivered and my face went pale with terror.

I held up the joint. “So this should settle them?”

She laughed. “Oh, no. You’re gonna have a bad time tonight, probably. But at least with this we can say goodbye to everyone before you break down.”

I could feel the color draining from my body. “What do you mean?”

“Someone inside wants to tell their story tonight, and I think they’re done asking nicely.”

“I… wow. Okay.” I took a deep breath. “I don’t know if I’m ready for that.”

“I don’t think you have a choice,” Lexi said. “Breathe. That kid deserves to be heard. It’ll be easier if you give up control voluntarily.”

Nodding, I took a deep breath. Then my grip on reality was flushed down a long, thin pipe into the recesses of my conscience, unable to see, hear, or know what was talked about that night.


## Zoe, 2021 (CW CSA)

I started to choke.

I was on the couch with Lexi and I felt afraid. We tried to talk but words wouldn’t come; we breathed in tight, gulping breaths, as if trying to breathe around something large in our mouth.

And I knew, I just fucking _know_ what I’m remembering. The same way I know how to breathe, or how adults ask to keep secrets. Scents and sounds assault my brain: musk, maleness, Bounty fabric softener on the white sheets, my screams muffled in a pillow, my hips up and bucking as he plugged my butthole and I cried and I cried and

Gods, he raped me.

He raped me in the master bedroom, while everyone else was away watching fireworks I was too afraid to watch.

He raped me in the trailer park and I screamed and nobody came to save me

he raped me

oh god no

## Tally, 2021

“Finally! I figured we’d get to you eventually.”

I came to lying on a stranger’s couch, my hands in hers. The cushion under my face was soaked in tears - more tears than I’d seen on under my eyes in my life. Despite that I felt nothing.

When I say nothing, to be clear, I mean “eerily calm.” I could turn off  panic by my will alone. Should I be concerned that I’m in an apartment I don’t recognize, around people I don’t remember, and crying? Probably. But I’m used to waking up in strange places.

I also needed to vomit for some odd reason, so that was new.

“What’s going on?”

“This is the tenth time you’ve asked that question,” Soli said. Soli! That meant I was at the Cats’ place, on their couch. In Seattle. Shit - was Addie late for a meeting? Best to wake her up and make sure—

“Let me get you some water. Sit up, please?”

Soli went to pull away but I lunged forward, despearte and childlike, to keep in contact. “Nono pwease don’t go pwease stay.”

The words came out hushed - almost a whisper - and with a heavy lisp. as if I couldn’t pronounce my “s” sounds all of a sudden. The horror of this dawned on me a moment later: I had speech therapy when I was in first and second grade.

*This system kid is stuck at six years old. My gods.*

“Can you breathe for me, sweetie?”

I tried my best to follow her breath but I felt a tickle at the back of my throat. “Stay with me,” Soli repeated, breathing in a slow, even pattern. I tried to match the cadence but my voice was vomiting forth something old, something ancient, something barely human.

What came out was a gut reaction, twisted over thirty years, that had grown into this mangled, barbed *thing* on the inside, and I could feel my memory burn with a truth I had forgotten for thirty-two years:

“Dwop it,” a tinny voice said from inside.

“Hi! Who are you—“

“No no nonono drop it drop it it’s not important drop it!”

“I know it’s a lot, sweetie. Breathe. You’re safe. It’s 2024; those people don’t know where you are.”

Then I was choking on nothing. 

“I told them he put his penis in me and they said I should drop it drop it you’ll forget anyway so dwop it.”

My body collapsed on itself for comfort. Soli tossed us a stuffie and we fell onto it like a life preserver at sea.

“Tally? You still there?”

Talking felt like a great effort; as if I was trying to control my body via an awkward remote. “Yeah. I don’t know this kid yet.”

“Does he have a name?”

“Dropper,” I said, not realizing the name would come out of my mouth. “He has memories of telling adults about being raped and locking himself in a bathroom for safety.”

“Holy shit.”

“Yeah, seriously.” A shudder passed over my body and I was the kid again, squeezing her hand ever harder. “Please don’t leave, Soli. Please, please, please!”

She flashed the kind of wide-eyed smile one might use to calm down a child. “I’m not going anywhere! Promise. Can I talk to Tally again? I just want to make sure you’re super safe sweetie!”

I floated somewhere between reality and unreality for a while, willing my body to listen to the commands I gave it. It took some doing before I could speak words again.

“Back,” I said. My brain was reacting to the constant switches by developing a massive headache. “Does this part ever get better?”

“Once you integrate, yes. But that requires orienting the others to the present.” She paused before asking the next question:

“How many of you are in there, anyways?”

I shot a shy smile. “You’ll tell them.”

“You have to tell the others eventually.”

“Fine.” I took a second and tallied with my fingers. “Four.”

“Four?” She laughed. “Yeah _right_. You’re definitely more than four. Fourteen parts? Forty parts?”

“Four hundred.”

A quiet unease filled the room.

“What happened to you?” Soli asked at last.

“I don’t know,” I said. “Apparently, I was supposed to drop it.”

## Twi, 2021

Was this the right way to do it? That’s for someone else to decide. But being around that many systems, in that close a proximity, for even a short period of time had me jumping headfirst into trauma processing.

Not that I had much of a choice in the matter; the kids made all the decisions that weekend. They decided to trust the Seattle crew. They opened up about their traumas because of that trust. And in many ways, knowing what happened was the first step to processing it. Even if it did leave me twitchy and chock-full of untreated PTSD symptoms.

I had no clue how this was going to shake out, but I trusted my gut. I felt relieved to know what was wrong with me, if not disgusted in what I learned. And I was talking to the other parts, finally. Things were supposed to start turning rosy from here, right?

…right?

I thought long and hard about this on the flight back to Chicago. Little did I know that this little system vacation was going to precipitate a complete change to my way of life, but for the moment I contented myself with memories of that big, safe space full of trans folk and plural systems who took in a broken Chicagoan trans girl and nursed her back to something resembling health.

# Hard Candy Christmas

## Twi, 2021

A week at compass comprised of twelve sessions of group therapy, five meditation sessions, and two individual sessions with a primary therapist. Ours was named Laurie, and let me tell you, she blew our mind near the end of the program. See, Laurie is an expert in dissociative disorders and child sexual abuse, and she was about to talk to Zoe without an adult present.

(…God, the strangeness of this never gets old. What in the world am I saying? “Without an adult present”? What does that even mean? No wonder I’m so fucked up—)

Addie, stop. Remember, Boundaries.

So I sat in group therapy, ready to name my emotions and my current mood level, when Laurie opened the door. We had planned this out earlier in the week after a really bad flashback; try as we might, the kids refused to talk unless the adults gave up the front entirely.

The idea terrified us. Zoe blocked us out completely whenever the flashbacks happened, as if trying to hold back a tidal wave on her own. Letting go of the relative security and safety of ignorance was, well, much like diving off a cliff and hoping the cove below had enough water to catch you.

Playing pretend at this disorder was fun while it lasted in Seattle. Sure, I pretended to be a kid back then because I got attention, obviously. But things are different now. there are *stakes*. We have to be certain that this system thing is real. That these memories aren’t, like, psychosis or hallucinations or smoking too much weed or something and—

And…

…And you know what, Addie, fine. You want to control the script, you write this part.

## Addie, 2021

I didn’t mean to—

…shit. Okay, I deserved that. Back when I was at Compass I wanted any excuse to avoid admitting the system existed. To say that I acted like a jerk to the system is an understatement.

Yes, I know this is silly. (We had even started writing this book at that point, so there was a *mountain* of evidence proving the system’s existence, but I digress.) But the one thing in this world I feared most was accepting Zoe’s truth.

If she wasn’t an alter, I was simply making up a tall tale about having a system.

If the system didn’t exist, the trauma wasn’t real.

If the trauma wasn’t real, the problem was completely internal, and therefore my fault. I’m hallucinating to get attention.

If I was hallucinating, I could continue to ignore that horrible things like that actually happen to People Like Me. It was destitute children from other countries being held in, I don’t know, a drug warehouse or something, someplace remote where I didn’t have to think about adults molesting children as a real, concrete thing that could happen to a person.

“Did you bring him?” Laurie asked. I jumped; the voices inside were so loud that I could barely hear her. But she stayed there in that moment with me, gentle voice and empathizing eyes, until I regrounded.

I pulled Jack-Jack from the backpack with a bit of a frown; a child inside was angry that we hadn’t let his head out of the pack so he could breathe. I heard a few heads turn as I cuddled the stuffie tight to my chest and followed Laurie to her office. The less I thought about what they were thinking, the better.

I imagined everyone staring at me at that moment, a plus-size woman in her late thirties clutching a stuffie and rocking with it like a child. They’d remember this, and I’d never live it down-

Right. Grounding. Exhaling, I released the stuffie from my bear hug and joined Laurie in the hallway.

“I’m nervous,” I said.

“Don’t worry. I prepared the space for her. Come inside.”

What was once a muted, dim therapy office with tastefully nerdy decorations had been transformed into a pediatric therapy room. Sensory jars filled with pink glitter and green slime tempted me from a side table. A pile of coloring books sat on an adjoining chair, complete with bright markers for coloring. I took a seat in the same chair I always chose and I swear to you, the chair was two sizes larger in that moment.

She readied her notepad and pen while I settled in.

“Nerves getting better?”

“A bit. Feeling silly, more than anything. But Misty keeps insisting I try. The alternative is more of, well, this *mess*.”

“Addie, I take it?”

“Ha,” I said, shaking my head in disbelief. “You’re good at that.”

“Addie, we’ve talked about this. It’s completely normal for systems to have little parts.”

“I know.” I picked up a sensory jar and twirled it. “And I’ve met them. They’re sweet. It’s just… I don’t like letting them out consciously, if that makes sense?”

She nodded. “Okay, Addie. Everything’s going to be okay. All of this is in the past and it can’t hurt you. The kid just wants to tell their story.”

“That’s not the hard part. It’s, uh,” my eyes darted to the floor.

“You’re safe.”

“I’m not used to uh, feeling little. Vulnerable. In need of protecting, you know? And especially around strangers, no offense.”

“This is what you came here for,” she reminded us. “To listen to them. To let their story be told. And you’re doing a really brave thing to listen to the others.”

“Yeah. Right, okay. Right.” I took a deep breath and tried to, well, blink out of existence. There wasn’t a good way to explain the sensation of trying to crawl out of your own body so that someone else could take control.

“You okay?”

“Uh, fine. Performance anxiety.” I could feel my cheeks flush with the childlike desire to play hard to get, to encourage an adult to see me and talk to me and make me feel safe.

I pursed my lips and concentrated. Nothing happened. I looked to her for a favor.

“Uh, it helps if you call her out.”

“Can I talk to Zoe?” Laurie asked, and I blacked out.
 

## Twi, 2021

Addie and I were cast out of consciousness and into a dark abyss.

We’re… nowhere, I guess. Picture a dead mall with the lights off. The smell of dust and musty piping filled the air. We were everywhere; we were nowhere; and in the core of it all was an angry, hissing *thing* that lived in the decrepit pit.

A dim projector played what looked like grainy home video against the white shutters of a closed store. In the image was a large church basement with wood paneling. Cameras and studio lights drowned the space in blindingly bright light and the air of an official studio.

A single figure stood behind the projector, shifty and shadowy. It was an angry pencil sketch that resembled a child, its face shifting and hissing with the flip of paper, the scratch of graphite, and sobbing. It shambled toward us with long, lurching steps, and when it opened its mouth a long, low hissing tone came forth to fill us with dread.

Above us I saw the real world, but it’s distorted. Like I was looking up through the thin skin of a drum and from there I could barely make out the therapist’s office. If I craned my ears I could hear the sound of a woman talking to what seems to be a small child. When I tried to scream out for help, though, nothing happened.

“The fuck is that?” Addie asked.

“It looks sharp and very, very painful,” I replied. “We should avoid it.”

The animated scribble continued its march toward us. The projector clicked through different scenes. Suburban home basement. A trailer by a creek. The church, again. Each image filled me with dread.

I could hear the scribble sharpening itself as it came closer, its jagged edges honed into light, tight, smooth-slicing killing machines. Addie scrambled for cover in panic, trying to find a weapon to defend herself.

I tried to be brave for the system. “Hey, we’re all friends here,” I started, but as I said that the scribble lashed out and left a long, then cut on my chin. I stumbled back and it lurched forward, sometimes at a crawl and sometimes at a dead sprint, its hissing now resembling a growling, a snarling, a cornered dog pockmarked with pustules and engorged ticks, its eyes alight with the will to live, its muscles giving out—

Lilith’s voice chirped in on the intercom. “Don’t run, you dummies. Remember what Laurie said: this can’t hurt you! Just listen, like we listened to you both!”

So I relaxed just like they taught me to do in therapy groups. Eyes closed, mind open, as the scared sketch charged at me with all its might, teeth bared, its eyes firmly affixed on the my jugular, and I leaned back so it could have a better shot at my throat.

“Do what you have to,” I said, and the sketch paused with its hashings brushing my neck.

## Twi, 2021 (CW Intense CSA)

Around me, the mall gave way to liminal space. I sailed on a turbulent lake covered in liquid nitrogen. Then: a cosmic flush, a great gurgling sound that swallowed the dark, the dawn, the celestial bears. I smelled fresh corn silk perfumed with cow shit on late country nights. My non-body flowed with stardust and sewage through a black hole that pulled and pulled until my atoms gave way to Higgs bosons—

When the ride stopped I was looking at a living room from my past. I was visibly myself again, and for a moment I wasn’t sure if I was the cosmos or if I was Twi, witnessing something so horrendous that it had been locked away for 30 years.

I was staring into a living room where a man and a child were watching a movie. Someone had scribbled over the face of the man and the child in my memory. In fact, everything in the room, from the color of the sofa to the pile of the carpet, feels like it’s just… deleted from my memory. There’s a laserdisc player on top of a giant rear-projection TV. They’re watching, uh, a Disney movie I think. Something with a cat.

The scribble reappeared, waved, and took his place on the couch. Frosty glass separated us, giving the whole scene a veneer of unreality.

Addie reappeared by my side and we watched as the child waited patiently on the couch, alone.

“Want to know something weird?” Addie asked.

“Hit me.”

“I feel like I’m on the other side of this glass. Like, I know this memory isn’t mine, but I remember everything about it. The way his couch was really cozy, and how he had a big sound system and a laserdisc player. But here I am, sitting on the outside looking in.”

“It is our memory,” I corrected her.

“Yeah.” She looked away. “Just wanted to try avoiding it one more time.”

The scribble was joined by an adult on the couch. He sat close to the scribble, slung his arm around the scribble, and pointed to the TV. “Want to sit on my lap and watch a movie?”

“Yeah!”

The scribble, Addie, and myself all said it in unison. I felt like a puppet on strings as I was lifted up, up, high above the glassy amnesia wall, until I was in the living room myself.

“You here too?” Addie asked.

“I think so.”

“I don’t trust this.”

“We know it’s going to suck,” I said. If Addie and I weren’t in the same body I would have given her a hug. “But that’s going to have to be okay.”

We felt small in the moment. Excited to please the nice guy on the couch. He’s nice to me, and he spends lots of time showing me movies and playing games.

Sitting in his lap feet weird. He had a weird, uh, _thing_ that moved when I sat on him. I tried to sit on one of his legs instead of over the, uh, thing, but every time I do he puts me back right in the center.

“We can watch your favorite movie,” he whispered in my ear. It made me feel special and loved, even if the thing in his pants made me feel funny.

“Hey, let’s play pretend. You be a kitty, and I’ll give you lots and lots of pets! Sound fun?”

“Yeah! I like cats! Meow!” I mimicked the movements of a kitty, toddling on all fours and pretending to clean my face. He started petting me like a cat: first on my head and then back and belly and then his hands went down down down—

## Twi, 2021

## Misty, 2021

I was in Laurie's chair with no clue how I got there.

From the looks of her full notebook we had just wrapped up a session. Did I miss something? My head was foggy, as if I had just come back from a nap. I gave the room a quick once-over to retrace my steps and noticed the coloring book on my lap. Coloring book? I *hated* coloring!

I laughed - when I'm nervous and disoriented my first reaction is always to laugh - and tried to shift my weight around.
 
My muscles ached. I had gigantic knots in my shoulders from too much tension, stiffness in legs wound tighter than clock springs, and tears. So, so many tears.
 
Laurie handed me a slip of paper and a tissue box while I got my bearings.

"That was Samantha. She asked for some things during our session: markers, ice cream, and a marble run set. I promised her I'd hand the note to the adults when we were done talking."
 
"Wait. I thought we just started our session!"
 
"We've been here for 90 minutes, Misty. You probably don't remember."
 
"Sorry," I said on reflex. Sorry was my default state most days. Sorry to have taken up space, sorry to have bothered you, sorry to have taken time off work because couldn’t be trusted to not kill myself.

If anything, sorry was easier than admitting how crackers and crayons crazy I was in that moment. From the outside I must have looked absolutely looney: a grown woman brings a stuffed animal to group therapy, disappears into a room, and regresses into a literal child that colored and remembered and—
 
”Say thank you."

Laurie’s sharp voice pulled me back to the real world.

“What?”
 
“To Samantha,” she continued. “Thank her. Spoil her tonight. She’s very brave to have shown up today.”
 
I felt my heart race, my vision blur.

"It's okay. Stay here with me, okay? You're safe."
 
I took a deep breath and tried to stretch. I felt cool as a cucumber, despite experiencing feelings of intense terror and shock. I cycled through the two emotions so quickly that I got to feeling nauseous.

“Close your eyes, Misty. Breathe for me.”

I did. The room came into focus, though my stomach wasn’t settled. “I guess she told you a lot, by the look of that notebook."

“Do you want to know?”

“I have to,” I said after a hard swallow.
 
She leaned back into her chair and folded her hands in her lap. Silence. She adjusted her glasses, met my eye, and laid me low with the truth:

“Samantha remembers being taken from school to be raped by other adults. You were groomed, trained, and then used.”
 
My breath caught in my throat. Another chuckle. "Okay. So, pretty bad then."

She leaned closer to me. “What you did was not your fault and was never your fault. These groups survive by discretion. You're only invited in if you're known, vetted, and provide material. Videos, pictures, or in some cases, children to abuse."
 
"That's not possible."
 
"Samantha has no reason to lie."
 
"I just..."
 
"I know this is hard--"
 
"I don't remember any of this."
 
She sighed. “You know you have amnesia barriers, Misty.”

"Who did it?"
 
“Your uncle.”

A dozen fragmented images danced across my memory. Each one contained a flashbulb of a weekend with him: a trip to the arcade, then a night alone at his house, where we’d play games together—“

“Hey, Stay with me here. You’re safe. None of the people who hurt you know where you are right now.”

I picked up a sensory jar from the table beside me and gave it a shake and did some shape breathing. I hated this part of having DID; whenever we got overwhelmed we had to stop everything and ground. Made us like a fainting goat, sometimes.

“Back?”

“Yes,” I said. “Thank you.”

I kept breathing and counting seconds: in for two, hold for two, out for two. In, hold, out. I felt the panic in my head calm down.

“Did my parents know?"
 
Her face hardened.

"Probably. It would be extremely unlikely for something like this to happen without some signs appearing here and there. Many times, in situations like this, parents are in on it or know about it in some way or another.”

That’s all it took to get the tears rolling.

I started crying like I had never cried before. It felt like someone had shoved a shiv deep into my heart, and the tears bled forth until they became streams, streams into rivers. I imagined crying infinite tears into an endless ocean that covered everything it touched with filth, shame, and guilt.

Laurie just waited while I drained an ocean of pain, offering reassurance from her chair. “I’m so, so sorry for what happened to you. It's wrong and should never have happened. But you can care for those kids now. You can help them heal."
 
I heard the implied message under her kind, unoffensive words: "And if you want to get better, you're going to have to share your life with them.”

## Addie, 2024

You know, in a way I was a perfect victim.

Neglected and abused by family to the point of being love-starved and desperate? Check. Dissociated to the point of forgetting where I was from time to time? Check.

And most importantly, I was literally blind to the men and women who raped me, so even if I did remember I couldn’t get a positive ID if I tried.

I hate his guts, but Uncle J was nothing if not smart. If you get one thing from this book, remember this: pedophile networks are filled with smart people who, above all else, _never want to be caught_.

And out in the sticks, where law enforcement is thin and social support networks are frayed, they could get away with almost anything their heart desired.

Fuck, man. We’ve been caged. Choked on dicks more times than we can count. Threatened with a knife to the throat to keep our rapists’ secrets safe. Hell, we were forced to film child porn with two girls, all of us naked and writhing on the shag carpet of a Sunday school classroom.

I wish I could remember their names, their faces, literally anything about them. Because we cared for each other, and we supported each other as the endless line of johns raped us without a care in the world, safe in the knowledge that they’d never be caught.

## Twi, 2021

On the last day of Compass I had a discharge session with Laurie. We’d spent the past few weeks talking about what happened; namely, I kept trying to convince myself that this couldn’t have happened, and Laurie would calmly explain how wrong I was. Eventually I came to a grudging acceptance of what happened: my once-favorite uncle used his position and his standing in the community to sell my body for sex.

Mostly, I was angry. Angry that my “close” hometown allowed something like this to happen. Angry that nobody in my blood family believed me. Angry that I was almost 40 and had to spend the past three months of my life in therapy. Angry - and honestly, just sort of *sad* - that evil like this actually existed in the world, and that my disorder was incontrovertible proof of its existence.

Angry at the thought of child pornography existing with my body in it, that I was some creep’s spank bank material and nothing more-

I paused, and took a deep breath. “Sorry, Laurie,” I said, pointing to my head. “Noisy inside today.”

“You’re okay!

But something else brewed inside me, and on that final session I said the words out loud:

“Laurie, I don’t want to die anymore.”

She stopped taking notes for a moment to meet my eye. “What makes you say that?”

“I’ve always wanted to die,” I continued. “Never knew why. But now, I guess, I know what caused me to want that, and I know that this isn’t how I want to beat them.”

“Beat them?”

“Messy phrase, I guess. I’m thinking of narrative therapy here, and figuring out what this means to me. Did I get that right?”

“Pretty on point,” Laurie said.

“Before, I thought dying was the way to finally make them see my pain. That maybe if I killed myself my family would finally believe what I told them as a child. But now? Knowing what all those monsters did to me? Laurie, I don’t just want to live. I want to spite those fuckers. I want to fix my life. I want to have hobbies again. I want to feel joy, fun, love.”

“And your friends and family?”

“I’m still scared,” I admitted. “Opening up seems really scary. If they know things about my past, they can use those facts to hurt me.”

“Do you think they will?”

“It has happened in the past,” I replied. “With my transness, my blindness, my faith, my trauma. You name it, I’ve opened up about it and faced the wrath of my abusers. Even in Chicago stuff still finds its way to their home, and if I give them the chance they’ll get their claws into my new family and ruin it too.”

“Do you really think your friends would believe your parents over you? After all they’ve seen, and all they’ve experienced alongside you for the past ten years?”

“Honestly? No. They don’t care enough about me anymore to do cruelty. But the kids inside still think about it a lot. I… Well, *we* ruminate on it a lot.”

I looked to the ground. Laurie pointed her pen in my direction. “I appreciate the honesty,” she said.

My fingers traced idle circles in the therapy chair. “What if I tell people about this and they show up at my doorstep, demanding I change my story?”

“Have they ever?”

“No,” I admitted. Breathe. We’re not being chased by a tiger; we’re in a safe therapy room. Practice the skills. “I guess I worry. It’s one thing to say they were bad parents. But this? This is a lot, Laurie. Nuclear-level revelations.”

“Yes, they are. And you should be prepared for whatever they feel they need to do to protect themselves. Nobody wants to be painted as a child molester, Twi.”

“That makes sense.”

“I’ve worked on both sides of the bars on this,” she continued. “With victims and perpetrators both. And the perpetrators know the stakes. That’s why these rings are so well organized, and they know how to, well, ‘process’ a kid to make them easier to abuse without discovery.

“It’s a wicked business, and they ensure silence by demanding every new member bring material or kids so they also have skin in the game. But that’s all to say, the last thing your family will want to do is touch this history.”

I chuckled. “That’s kind of comforting, actually. Never thought I’d say that about CSA.”

“The tiniest silver lining! Right, I’m quoting that in your safety plan: ‘live for spite.’ What about the kids?”

“I’ll buy them more toys and make space for them to have their own experiences. It’s hard, and I feel really silly about doing it in front of others. But I’ll try.”

“Good.” She paused for a moment. “I want you to know, I’m *proud* of you. You’ve something extremely hard and should feel some pride for that.”

Having a safe, well, “adult” say that made the kids inside beam with pride.

“Thank you,” I said, holding back some tears. “It means a lot coming from you.”

## Lilith, 2025

Looking back, the silliest part of my suicide attempt is that we almost died over a bad karaoke song and too many McChickens.

No, seriously. We were working at one of those pre-pandemic tech startups that offered free booze and lots of activities in the office. One Thursday after work a bunch of us stayed at the office to get drunk and play karaoke.

We thought about leaving early to avoid the happy hour, but then people insisted that we have a drink, and one became two, two became four, and karaoke happened, and... well. You know. Drunk people order 200 bucks of McDonalds on the corporate card and ruin their diets to sate their drunk needs. If nothing else, the death poem would have been hilarious:

> > Too drunk and too sadened;
> > While my friends sing “Aladdin”
> > and eat McChicken,
> > In Lady Michigan my life will be stricken.

I almost killed myself over nothing. _Nothing_.

We aren’t proud of this. In fact, it may be one of the worst nights of our lives. But it’s the first time we knew that we wanted to live, and it’s the first time we talked to each other. So here we are, mentioning it in the book, because we promise you, this memoir is going to show warts and all.

# 10

# It Pays to Belong

## Addie, 2021, December 31, 11:55PM
The night I graduated from Compass was New Years’ Eve, and D and I were in no condition to celebrate.

They had recently undergone top surgery and were still recovering. (I think they still had the wound drains installed, actually!) My back was still killing me, so I laid on a yoga mat in the middle of our living room for the remainder of the night.

I fingered my graduation present for getting through Compass: a tiny necklace charm, chosen by the staff to reflect our journey. Ours was a small captain’s wheel, symbolizing that we were now in control of our destiny. (I still have it hanging over my desk, actually.) At the time the cheap little pewter charm meant more than, well, most anything in my life.

Around us, the house was in disarray. Our water heater blew out the day after Thanksgiving, flooding our entire kitchen. A demolition crew came through to save the house from water damage, but as a result we had a destroyed kitchen and were doing dishes in the bathroom sink.

Yeah, to say we were a mess is an understatement.

We sipped cheap champagne from plastic flutes we had purchased for a time when parties were still allowed and could still turn into a rager. Silence filled the room as the clock ticked toward midnight. We wanted to be sleeping, not pretending to enjoy a party.

“We need a vacation,” D said, and I couldn’t have possibly agreed more.

## Potts, 2017
Our family winter cabin vacations started as an escape from the rat race.

We were all in our early thirties at the time and the pressures of pre-pandemic office life were getting to us. Between going into the office every day, evening happy hours with work friends, and other little events that came up through the week, we were all exhausted.

We landed on a winter cabin vacation for two major reasons:

1. There were no good vacations between January and March, so we wanted to invent something to cut through the boredom.
2. This cabin had a fireplace and us city slickers were *determined* to have a raging fire going the whole time we were there.

Getting there required a seven hour drive and a walk down a snow-covered, icy hill. The end product, though, was a wooden hunting lodge with beautiful views of an ice-covered lake. The only thing ahead of us was four uninterrupted days to hang with our chosen family, with no internet, plenty of beers, and a mutual understanding that this vacation was about resting.

Over the past seven hours we had discussed gamifying the vacation, just to make sure we were inspired to relax. We awarded points for doing self-care and the highest score “won” the vacation. Look. We’re a whole gaggle of gay nerds, what else did you expect?

“So did we ever finalize out our point system for the cabin? I think naps are fifteen points, for sure. Doing legitimate self care is five points. If you do finish some big project, that’s like fifty points, right?”

Outside, it was so quiet that you could hear branches creaking a mile away. I could hear my heart pulsing, the wind whipping, and the soft crunch from a rabbit making tracks a few feet away.

Brandon busied himself with unloading firewood from the back of the SUV. “Those numbers sound good, D. The most important thing is that we rest and have a slow, fun, long weekend!”

The others handed me a case of beer and I started to make my way down the hill. It didn’t take long for me to find a patch of ice, though. My footing gave way, but I thought I could ride out the skid. You know, like a kid on a sled, except my sled would be my two useless boots.

That didn’t work out, of course, and a little pebble on the hill sent me flying. Beer cans flew everywhere and my back hit the ice with a satisfying “thud.” I could hear hissing as a few of the cans popped open, turning the fresh snow yellow.

A small part of me - something old, unknown, and terrified - jumped to the front, ready to take whatever abuse we were about to get. Growing up I was a clumsy kid because, well, *low vision*, and that usually led to long shouting matches whenever I broke things.

But here in the present, my family made sure I was okay. They took care of the rest of the hauling so that I could rest. Later, we even laughed about it.

Welcome to a scrapbook of the Arcades’ family vacation.

## Addie, 2021
“You’re only allowed to go down the hill once this year,” Brandon said. “No more accidents!”

I came prepared that year with crampons and a general understanding that the half-blind woman shouldn’t go up and down an icy hill more than absolutely necessary. This time, my job was to get the fire and ambience going while the others hauled things into the house. It made the space feel cozy immediately, which was great after a seven hour car trip.

The crampons did their work and this was the first year since our first time here that I got down without eating it on the ice. A sign, maybe, that things were going to go smooth this year. We felt so confident that the worst was behind us, now that Compass was out of the way. We planned hard for this vacation, too. Brought crayons Jack-Jack for the kids, music making stuff for Twi, games for the adults. We had a whole catalog of grounding strategies, a veritable bounty of thought interruption strategies, and a positive, forward-thinking attitude toward the trauma. Hanging out away from home for four days was going to be a breeze after all this therapy!

That year’s trip also happened a few months after I graduated from the PHP. I wore my little captain’s wheel charm close to my chest, and when I felt like I wasn’t good enough to get through this weekend without a flashback I’d tap it to remind me - us - of how far we’d come. That we could get through an entire weekend and have fun, no flashbacks, if we relied on our therapy skills.

We put on some Carole King on a bluetooth speaker for background music and set about my job of firemaking. Everyone inside my head agreed: we were going to have a great time. Some of the kids wanted to help with the fire so we talked them through building a basic fire. “You start with rumpled newspaper, then sticks, then a log or two. The paper lights the sticks, and the sticks light the log!”

Soon, we had a tiny fire going in the fireplace.

The second that smoke touched my nose, though, I knew I was going to fall out during this vacation. You just get a feel for these things, with time. Can’t control when they happen, but if you learn to look for the signs you can certainly get a flashback forecast.

D’s freezing hand touched my shoulder and the shock of the touch brought me back to my body. “Everything OK inside? You were just staring off into the distance there.”

I shook my head. “I was raped in a place just like this, once. Lakeside property and everything.”

They flashed a look of both sympathy and dismay. “Shit. I’m sorry hon. Are you gonna be okay?”

“I don’t know,” I lied. “No. It’s gonna be flashback town. I just… fuck, D. I didn’t want this to crash into my friends. I don’t want them to see me like this.”

“They all *love* you, hon. You will be fine.”

“I know. It’s hard to feel that, though.”

## Samantha, 2022 (TW CSA)

I opened my eyes and teleported to 1989.

There were adults in the other room playing cards. Their voices sounded like something warm and fuzzy, but the room smelled like beer and gin and smoke. When I smell those things the bad things are going to start happening. It started with the smell, then, the scent of his Seagrams mixed with the moustache as he leaned down to kiss me—

“They’re safe, little one.”

It was a voice I knew from inside my head but in the moment I had lightning in my veins. The man with the black moustache and the whiskey lived in a house like this one. I woke up there once, dazed and sore, after he did bad things to me.

Was I back there again? I couldn’t chance finding out. I was in a great room with no good lines for hiding. But if I went to the bathroom and was sneaky, I could slip out the front door and get help.

“You are alone in the woods with good friends, Samantha. Please calm down. You’re having a flashback!”

I really really wanted to run away. To run and run and find someone who could listen and I could just tell them, _tell them I’m being raped_, it could all be over. But the voice inside was gentle and told me I couldn’t do that. It’s feminine. Loving. Motherly.

“You can’t leave,” she said.

“I want to be alone.”

“Okay. we can do that, little one. Just a moment.”

The world went all fuzzy in that way that makes me want to forget the world. My body moved without me having to think about it, which was really weird. I felt like I was riding on my own shoulder and that was kind of fun.

“Going somewhere?” the boring adults asked.

“Getting a beer,” my voice said, but it wasn’t my voice because it was an adult. The others went back to their game and we slipped through the kitchen to an attached solar. It was dark and there were some hiding places there.

“Safety through obscurity,” Tally said. “You can hide in here if you want. You’re safe.”

I tucked in behind a chair just in case it really was 1989 and the bad man was still looking for me. And then I breathed really really shallow because I didn’t want anyone to hear my breath or give anything away. This never worked, but it gave me a little control over the when..

If you had time you could prepare. Cry a bit, try to get into the place where pain doesn’t hurt anymore and the world is a dull, grey haze. At least this way I’d hear his footsteps in the hall and have a few seconds to prepare.

“Has anyone seen Austen?”

There’s another voice in the other room that sounds familiar. Big friend D. They’re married to the adults in here and when I’m out they act like my big friend and get me stuffies and coloring books and stuff but anyway, yeah, that helped a little bit. They’d fight the bad man if he was here.

Today was a crying day but I had to be quiet because the adults were up and looking for me. I curled up into a tiny little ball and let the tears flow as quietly as I could. They’d find me soon enough, and they’d tell me to stop being a baby about it. But if I cried hard now, I wouldn’t cry when they saw me, and that helped a lot.

D said something else and sounded sad: “Oh god, they’re gone.”

A new voice. A man. Shivers run down my spine.

“I’ll check outside.”

This was it! I curled up tight and checked my sight lines and did my best to play dead. As a final touch I grabbed a quilt from a nearby rack and threw it over myself so I could pretend to be a pile of blankets.

The adults came into the solar once, twice. Each time they missed me. If I hid long enough I could sneak out later, when they were all sleeping. Then the bad man wouldn’t get me.

“Oh gods,” D said. “Last time this happened was the beach incident.”

“We’ll find them.”

I knew I was made when the third adult checked the solar. Our eyes met. In mine, he could see a raw, feral child, shirking from the light, trying desperately to not be seen.

D rushed in with a stuffie and the tears really started to flow. “I’m glad we found you, little one!”

“I’m so sorry,” I said in a shrill, childish voice.

D hugged me. “We’re all just glad you’re safe! Come on. It’s 2022 and we’re with our family at the cabin. Can you take some deep breaths for me? Come on. In, and out.”

I did what they wanted me to do and then fell apart. A dam inside me burst and the tears came out in long, gaping wails. “I wasn’t supposed to come out in front of friends.” I apologized, again and again and again so D wouldn’t be mad at me.

I didn’t want them to see this side of me. The mess. The inner child, terrified and alone, no longer able to care for herself. There was silence, maybe a bit of murmuring, in the other room. Someone inside reacted to the sound with intense shame.

“One of the bad men lived in a place like this.”

“Do you need to check and make sure he’s not here?”

I nodded. “I don’t wanna do it alone.”

“No, no, nonono!” they pet my hair. “Sweetie, I’ll be with you the whole time. I promise! If anyone’s there, I’ll fight ‘em.”

“And you’re a lawyer, so you’d win.”

“…in court!” They took my hand and gently led me to my feet. Familiar faces waited in the kitchen beside the solar, their worried eyes filling me with shame.

“This is Samantha, and she’s nine. She wants to make sure there are no bad men here. Is it okay if we search the house and make sure of that?”

There was a second where I thought the silence would stretch on forever. But no, it took only a moment for Brandon to reply. “Of course! Samantha’s welcome to check every room if she wants. I’m sorry you’re having a rough night. Would you like anything while you’re here?”

D nudged me. “The adults got you dino nuggies.”

“Those please,” I said. Brandon laughed and fiddled with the oven. D walked me around the entire house and there wasn’t a bad man there at all.

I ate a whole big plate of dino nuggies and said hi to the family. They were all really nice and totally got that I wasn’t an adult. And it felt good to just be out and not feel like the adults had to be ashamed of me.

Anyway goodbye now

## Addie, 2022

I came to with a full belly and tears streaming down my face. My entire body screamed with cramps and aches, as if I’d just spent the past hour terrified of the cabin—

Right. It’s DID. It’s _always_ DID getting in the way, isn’t it?

Phil saw me switching and grabbed my attention. “Hey there friend! Who’s up now?”

“Addie. I…” my voice trailed off. “Gosh. I didn’t want you all to see that. I’m so terribly sorry.”

“You’re fine,” Brandon said. “If it helps, this isn’t the worst flashback we’ve been around for.”

“Really?”

Mark chimed in. “Yeah, remember that you forget a lot of things friend!”

DID, man. This shit is a trip.

## Sam, 2023
Brandon, Phil, Mark, and D sat with around the fire with me, crossfaded, watching the sun set over the lake.

(And yes, I’m telling my part in the present tense if I want to, Twi. You should feel lucky I’m trying to help here anyways.)

“It’s funny.”

“What’s that?” Phil said. He sat on a wooden rocking chair, knitting in the dusk and firelight.

“That we can be published and have fans and all that.” The teacup in my hands radiated warmth, and its steamy wisps mixed with the smell of fresh wood smoke. I felt silly - no, I felt needy. That showing something beautiful I created is an overreach, a boundary break that I can never recover from if I bring it up.

But I’m trying to do better about it. Accepting that we’ve achieved things, that we’ve changed people’s lives, that we’ve _lived a consequential life_. And, most dangerous of all, that we should celebrate the achievements we’ve made in this life. That it’s okay to take pride in those accomplishments.

“So I got this letter a few months back.” I opened my computer and started searching. “Was on a platform I didn’t visit that often anymore. But I logged in to download some old files and there it was, this note in my mailbox from a fan. He read my book ten years ago, and took the time to find me and reach out after a reread a few months back.”

“You get fan mail?” Phil asked, incredulous.

“Here and there,” I said, not thinking about how that sentiment must look like on the outside. Fan mail? Hell, I was nominated for an Ursa Major award in high school. I’ve lost more fan mail than I can even count.

“I just didn’t really…, think about it, you know? That you’d get mail.”

“Yeah. I’ve been recognized at bars for my writing. Cons too. And I guess I have fanart hanging up in my house, too.

“I’ve had people tell me that my books changed the entire trajectory of their life. It’s an awesome responsibility.”

Phil shook his head. “Of course you would be that person.”

“Sorry. I don’t like to boast.”

“No, it’s beautiful!” he put down the knitting. “It’s just, I’m not surprised.”

Brandon chimed in from the other room: “I never expect you to have done everything, but I’m never surprised when you say you have done something.”

Laughing, I handed the laptop to Phil so he could read the letter. When he finished he handed the laptop back to me in silence.

“He has *headcanon*, Phil. *And he isn’t even the first one to have that.* Had I not looked at the mailbox I would have missed it entirely.”

He met eyes with me. That is one thing I really love about Phil; even though I can’t see it, he still manages to meet my eyes and make me feel seen.

“You changed that guy’s life,” he said.

“And I don’t remember writing the thing that changed his life. It’s like, like someone else wrote that book. It just *exists* without a creator. But there I am, reading this raw reminder that my writing has reach - has *power* - and I’m just thinking, ‘I don’t deserve this. Someone else made this happen.’ It bothers me.”

“Someone else did do that. In your head, that is.”

“Still. Fucking terrifying, to realize I have that kind of power inside of me.”

“And you use that power responsibly,” Phil said.

“Well, as responsible as a crazy, furry, queer lit writer can be.”

“The most responsible!” Phil said, raising his glass to toast.

## Twi, 2023

We were telling midnight tales around a roaring fire. Outside, the world is asleep save for the occasional snowmobiler buzzing by this corner of the lake. We were all crossfaded and in our feelings, talking about core fears and such, when I felt compelled to tell a story.

“So we’re walking around Gencon in Indianapolis, right? Big gaming con in my hometown. And some years, post-system discovery, that was a bad thing. You know, ghosts of my childhood hiding around every corner of this town. But the con itself’s super fun, worth a visit if you ever want to go. Anyway.

“I was wearing this silly catenby outfit that we jokingly called “A wizard sent their familiar to Bermuda as punishment but the catenby just turned it into a vacation” look, complete with overalls and a button-up and everything. It’s been, gosh. 8 years since I’ve transitioned and usually I can do this con without ever getting identified.

“But there I am, waiting outside the bathroom while D does their business, and this lady stops in front of me with this shocked face. ‘Austen?’ she asked, and then launched into a hug.

“I waggle my cane at her to be, well.” I pretend to wiggle a white cane in real life. “So when she let go I shout, ‘You gotta tell me who you are!’ and come to find out, she’s an old high school friend. Yet _I can’t remember her._ It’s like an itch you can never scratch, memory issues. But yes. Marion.

“I don’t know if I told y’all what all happened, but I’ve had some shit happen at this con. Mess, flashbacks, fallouts. Hell, Our mutual Craig still tells the story of the time I was exhausted and sleeping in the hall, and two moments later I was the loudest, most boistrous version of myself I could possibly be, as if a switch had flipped. Which, well. Obviously I know what that is now, but talk about forty foot tall burning letters screaming ‘YOU HAVE DID’, right?”

Phil nudged me. “Come on! Tell me about Marion.”

“Right. Well turns out she follows me on TikTok and knows about my transition and such. But she introduced me to her husband as ‘the smartest person she’s ever known.’ And I just think, that’s wild, right? Just saying that. Of course she’s met smarter people.”

“Actually…”

Brandon butted in. “We had this conversation yesterday,” he said with a laugh. “You’re stupid smart. Nobody here is surprised when you can just _do_ something none of us know how to do.

“And before you ask, because this is the next question on your queue, yes. We - the collective we, the queer fam - always know who is up. We just assume that you know we know.”

There were nods around the fire, and honestly that scared me a bit. It’s weird to be perceived, I guess.

## Addie 2023

On our way back from the cabin that year we stopped off  some small Wisconsin town near the Dells to wait out a snowstorm. Our attempt to get home was interrupted by a massive blizzard that would either have ended with a night at a hotel or our car in a ditch. I’m glad we chose the former.

To pass the time in the hotel room, Brandon and I chatted about work - or more specifically, the job market. I’d been hunting for a new gig for the past year, on and off, and getting no bites.

“It’s wild. I’m so used to just getting a job, you know? But i’ve had a dozen interviews and no hits. I actually think I’m getting _worse_ as I go about this.”

“That’s just imposter syndrome talking. I’m sure you’ll find something soon! Besides,” he added, leaning in for emphasis, “Didn’t you have that talk with workplace assistance? I’m sure your job will have to accommodate you in some way.”

“Yeah,” I said. “…yeah! It’s weird, though. Did I tell you what the company guy wanted to do?”

“No!”

“He wanted an appointment with my therapist to talk about my DID.” I shook my head. “I swear, I’ve never had an employer talk to my therapist in my entire life until this moment. I didn’t even realize it was a possibility! But yeah. He asked a lot less questions about vision and a lot more about more questions about the system thing, though.”

“That’s weird.”

“He said he’d never considered what was required for that before meeting me, so I’m helping him out a little bit. Which feels cool, I guess, if it helps another person going through this down the way.”

“When do you get news?”

“Monday, probably!”

“You’ll have to let me know how it goes,” he said, and we thought nothing more of it. We probably moved on to telling old work stories or something.

## Twi, 2023
### Why do you sign your pieces?
#### or: the “individuals vs. parts” question

I write this to you from a cozy cabin in Wisconsin. We’re surrounded by friends - family, really - and we’re simply enjoying each other’s company for the long weekend. Soon we’ll go back to Chicago, where we’ll learn what kind of accommodations our company is willing to offer for our eyes and our brain. I wanted to write this while we had a moment to reflect and process what’s happened.

The hill didn’t trip me up this year. Phil offered an arm and my white cane helped me check for ice patches. Inside, the fire roared and our friends stretched in front of it to relax muscles from a long car ride.

The thing is, this disorder kept on kicking the shit out of us.
Work only got harder. dates and deadlines started to slip. I’d spend most workdays staring at the screen, so overwhelmed at the sheer *volume* of work that my brain would just shut down. Or a kid would come out.

But we try to do things that make us happy, like writing.

As a system, preparing to write is like calming down a movie theater full of ten year olds. Can’t start the movie until everyone’s found a seat, settles down, and it’s quiet, after all! We’ve built up a system to get everyone settled in if we need to write something together. It’s one thing to write when you’re the only person in your head; it’s another thing entirely to draft a novel with hundreds of different people screaming in your ear. I would like to provide our ritual for that focus, free of charge, for any multiple who reads this:

First, there’s the Closing Of All Of The Tabs. Between Max and Twi’s coding, Samantha’s obsession with mechanical devices, Sam’s Amazon cart, and all the other wonderful interests you pick up along the way as a system, you end up with a lot of tabs.

“We should pick up juggling again!”

A child jumps up and down to get my attention. Lilith. She’s a sweetheart, even if she tries to be thorny about it. “We could do that, sweetie, but remember the rule about interrupting without permission?”

She scuffed her foot on the ground. “I have to go to the system chat and ask for time. I know.”

“Exactly.” I give her a quick pet on the head. “That get you what you need, girl?”

“…yeah.” Her eyes sparkled. “But you should keep this in the book. That way, anyone who reads it can be like, ‘hey, Arcades! you ever let Lilith do that juggling thing?’ And I’m guaranteed to juggle for life.”

“You know what? I’d absolutely do that with you, Lil! We could practice between meetings at work. Stand up and get active and all that!”

And… right. Where was I? Who was I? Am I still Twi? Nope, not Twi. Can’t remember the last hour of time passing, and apparently I’m doing some sort of freewrite for the book now?Fuck. It’s Potts.

Okay. I got this.

Potts here, taking whatever Twi had planned and doing it better:

A recipe for focus and writing
10 minutes | 1 serving

 1 | browser full of tabs
 3 | projects just sitting around
4-5 | video games the littles want to play
1 | playlist of music everyone can agree on

1. Close all the browser tabs. Save off things people want to remember into our notes.
2. Marvel at the number of silly little projects we start and how few of them ever get finished.
3. Negotiate with the system on post-writing activities. Add alter front scheduling to the calendar as necessary.
4. Repeat until mind calms.
5. Optional: Cannabis can be infused into the focus process for better flavor.

But yeah, Right. Give me a moment to look back.

Funny story how I got here, by the way. Twi made a gin and soda when drafting this section, and by that I mean he poured a single shot of gin, dissociated, and proceeded to clean our bong. Then, someone grabbed the gin, assumed we just wanted a shot for later, and came back upstairs. An hour later, though, I’m sitting here staring at this lonely shot of gin sitting on the counter, untouched.

But settling in for a group writing session, right! We wanted to uh, make an outline I think. Things that happened. Moments like this. Because the inside experience of system life is this incredible machinarium, full of fascinating conflicts and funny stories. We wrote this book to share our pain, but at the same time we want to show you the beauty of system life.

Yeah, it’s chaotic. Yeah, it’s screwed up. And yeah, this moment is going to come off as crazy to many, if not most people, who read this book. But I believe, deep in my heart, that the collective person on these pages is the only person I could have been in, the only way that it could have been, and instead of ending on the pain I want to show you the beauty and joy that come along with system life.

But when we sit down to write, and we’re all up and excited, everyone starts trying to write at once, and we end up with a manuscript that looks like it came from a serial killer or something…

Twi screamed from the back. “Don’t you dare pull out that ‘I’m too crazy’ crap, Addie. We’re not crazy. We’re different.”

My mind blanked for a moment. Eyes fluttered, vertigo swelled. Then I was Addie, in front and in full control.
